RATING: 6
Original rating: 4 - Dated: 2015-04-29
I should have known that games that require a dungeon master are not for me. Couple that with lots of fiddly rules and multiple aspects to keep track of with no real rhyme or reason, and you end up being one of the most dissapointing games I've played in awhile. I want to like this game, but its like the really cool looking bottle of wine or beer with a cool label, yet tastes terrible and leaves a bad taste in your mouth.

What I did like: the maps and minis. 

Everything else in this game either requires learning too many rules that aren't intuitive or waiting through significant scenarios for meager rewards. It's not as deep as the Star Wars RPG's I grew up playing, and it's not as fun as battling with the Star Wars minis. I'm sure i'm in the minority, based on the BGG rating, but this game just isn't what I thought it could be, and wasn't all that fun.

Updated Rating: 5 - Dated 2017-03-10
I am starting to soften on my stance with this game. Originally, I had rated this at a 2, but that was unfair. It was due mostly to having played the game (campaign) with two different groups, where in one, the DM was very unfair, leaving out explanations of rules, and not working very hard to correct obvious mistakes we made as Rebels, the other being a group where the two other players (one the DM, the other the Wookie) seemed content to have their own in-jokes, and own RPG-Lite going on, irregardless of me, or the campaign we were playing. That soured the game for me.

I updated the rating to a 4, because it was unfair to judge the game on the players I played with.

After having restarted the campaign (this time as the DM) with a group I enjoy playing with, the game is fun. But what I've already stated about fiddly rules are still true. The rule book comes out once a scenario, even after multiple plays. There are some things in the game that don't make intuitive sense, and in some cases, detract from the theme. 

I will, however, keep playing now that I've got a good group to play with. I'm also hopeful that the eventual app fixes some issues with how to build and maintain a campaign, and that the rules come a bit more naturally after multiple additional plays.

EDIT: updated rating to a 6 after playing many skirmish missions. This seems to be the "best" mode of the game - although the rules issues still plague this version of the game as well. If I were to make this game over, I'd strip out half of the rules requirements. Battles should be seamless and easy (similar to Memoir '44, for example).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.8
[thing=164153]Imperial Assault[/thing] (2p skirmish, 2-5-player campaign, 1-2 hours per mission)
There are two games in the box: the campaign game and one-shot skirmish mode (with 6 scenarios). After 2 skirmish games and 9 missions of the campaign, the game has been everything I have expected, although because this is the first game of this type to us, it should be easier to exceed my expectations.

Imperial Assault is extremely fun to play and the missions are varied (not just killing). A campaign has at least 11 missions, half of them side missions that vary between campaigns (random draw and rebels choose one from the 2+ available). The different weapons (attack dice) and defenses (defense dice) allow push-your-luck moments, and sometimes the dice are on you side and sometimes against you. You have an overall strategy, but the game is played in a very reactive, tactical way due to the alternating activations.

As the imperial player I cringe when I see the level-upped rebels take out my stormtroopers in one shot before they even get to activate on that round, but if the rebels take too long killing my troops, they neglect their objectives, and I come out on top again. And the rebels are not the only ones that get to have new abilities.

Also: I have painted the miniatures. Even a quick paint-job will make the game look amazing. (Unfortunately for a painter, every painted game needs to look better than the previous one, so a good job takes quite a while. Well, at least painting is a good peripheral hobby to go with boardgaming.)

Original:
What a waste of money in my
Opinion. I am a diehard Star Wars fans, TRPG DM & Player for years, and enjoyed both strategy and dungeon crawl games. I saw the reviews and the beautiful components, then I thought I must get this. I even paid more money to get expansion characters such as Han Solo and such.

I have to say, the only think I like is the miniature scuplys. I got the game for a few years and the user manual itself scared me. It was not well written and confusing, pointing you to jump from page to page. Surprisingly, the much more Harvey TRPG rule books were much more clear and good read.

Some might said that the game is very simplified. Indeed to compare to TRPG or hardcore streatgy. But I was hoping this game to be more simplified. To me, I think this is too complicated. If I have the time to play this, I might as well play TRPG. If I want good strategy, I might as well play Shogi or Eurogame, which simple yet with depth. If I want to play dungeon crawl, I would just go for D&D Advemture system anytime, which was simple enough to be customizable to make it more fun.

I played my first game of this with colleagues. Just figuring out how to rebuild the tutorial map took us such a long while. It felt like I played a puzzle building along with reading boring confusing rules to desicpher complicated code. Most disappointed game for me so far, as I have had high expectation...

RATING: 8
The only game that I have painted the miniatures for. That should show how much I enjoy it.

The joy of Star Wars distilled in a box. Many adventure games and dungeon crawlers can become over-full of book-keeping and die-modifications, to the point in which it can feel exhausting. Some players greatly enjoy this aspect of gaming. However, it just wouldn't feel right to have a Star Wars game with the same sort of barrier to entrance. I think Fantasy Flight has done a wonderful job of streamlining the dungeon experience and creating a game that heavily suggests the feeling of a Star Wars skirmish. The amount of gameplay and longevity is also incredible, supporting a campaign mode with enough story and progression to feel depth, as well as a fast-paced skirmish mode that is great for little one-offs. The support for the main game, through miniature expansions, is also admirable and the amount of content you receive in cards and skirmish scenarios justify the price and show that Fantasy Flight is creating win-win situations for the company and for fans.

RATING: 5
Another move and roll game.  Didn't seem to have any meaningful decisions, but this is based on only one play.  Also (blasphemy) I'm not that into the Star Wars theme.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Thanks a lot FFG for yet another crappy rulebook. Sure, it is true that the system has been streamlined from previous generations of dungeon crawlers (Descent and Doom), but the way it is presented to the players is rubbish. The rulebook has an onion structure distributing all essential information of a rather simple game over tens of pages.

Another weak point is the infinite levels of pain the setup induces. Sure the game is beautiful but the search for the right bits and pieces is a quest of abysmal frustration. Oh why could not you just make a pile of generic pieces instead of individual ones.

In the end we got sick and tired of the setup and trying to find the information we needed. Without giving it further thought we covered the gaps in the rules with our own and quickly grabbed a bunch of troops and cards to play with. Once the game finally started, it shone like only a star could. Take my word with a grain of salt though. After all those brain burners, a fast paced dice fest always welcome to my table. I do not mind if the game does not offer the cleverest of mechanics as long as offers a retreat to another universe.

If you fancy a game calling for deep thinking, skip this one. You will just ruin the experience with your analysis. But should you enjoy quick decisions based only on your intuition, you might find Star Wars Imperial assault a rather refreshing experience.

RATING: 10
I love the Star Wars theme.  This game is a 1 vs many game, where one player will play as the Imperials and the others play as the Rebels.  As the Empire, you will build your faction with various tools at your disposal.  The Rebels have a series of characters that will gain abilities as they advance through the non-linear story that is determined by several cards.

RATING: 8
Where X-Wing drops you into the cockpit of iconic Star Wars snubfighters and Armada puts you in command of entire fleets, Imperial Assault takes you right down to the adventures on the ground that are so central to Star Wars. Both sides are incredibly fun to play, with the ragtag band of heroes earning experience and accomplishing missions while the Imperial player deploys all the might of the Empire to stop them. Definitely one of my favorite games right now!

The box is also two games in one, and the skirmish ruleset is nothing to sneeze at.

RATING: 7
1st plays: This is a refined version of the Descent 2.0 system with a better theme.  I really wanted to like Descent, but the hand of take-that cards always felt jarring, and the long turns caused it to drag a bit.  Threaded actions and less gotcha's speed up the game and make for a positive experience.  I do wish there were more Jedi/Sith though.

2nd plays: We played this multiple times in one night, and the campaign was interesting, each scenario was different, and not quite the frantic pace of Descent.  It's still a race to complete objectives, but slightly less so, and still a very tight margin for error.

RATING: 8.7
FFG could have very easily taken the lazy rout and just slap Star Wars pictures recycled from their LCG on Descent and they would have made back more than their investment. I don't mean to sound cynical but that is exactly what Hasbro has done (for years) with Star Wars Monopoly or Star Wars Risk. FFG has shown love and care in this game, and it shows from the countless of flavor text written everywhere from the rulebooks to the hundreds of cards and the overall reworking of a tried and true game system. To put it bluntly: Imperial Assault is far from âDescent, but with Star Warsâ. It is a perfectly streamlined and designed game that takes classic design concepts and mechanics and refines them to one of the best gaming experiences I've ever had.

RATING: 8.8
Uses well-tested mechanics from games like Descent or even the FFG Star Wars RPG to provide an entertaining Campaign or Skirmish experience. A bunch of missions included right in the box, and the miniatures and other components look fantastic. Firing and melee rules are simplistic, and the opposed die rolls help to mix up what might happen during a combat rather than being a 100% sure thing. The line of sight is easy to follow, and Strain and Wounding mechanics help the Heroes feel...a little more heroic than the Imperial mooks they face. Dat AT-ST though. Pain to put the gun on the nose, but damn does it look good.

RATING: 8
Imperial Assault is two games in one. With a Star Wars setting. And wonderful miniatures. What's not to like?

At its core, Imperial Assault has the same engine of Descent 2nd edition, with some small tweaks. The most important, in the campaign mode, is the alternate activation of a hero and a group of villains: it creates, at the same time, quicker turns and lots of tactical tension.

The campaign mode looks like it will be my preferred gaming mode, but skirmish is somewhat appealing too... two great games in one!

RATING: 9.25
Rogue One in a box, basically.  Which to me is awesome!  Once you get the rules, the combat system is clean but has a ton of options with skills.  We have had some epic moments when I played through the campaign - even to the point of having the very last mission come down to one action in one turn (if I as the Imperial side had one more action, I would have won!).  I really like that in the campaign, both sides get to upgrade and have advantages, to the point that you can give the Rebel player(s) a great experience without holding back as the Imperials.  I haven't played skirmishes yet, but I can tell they are a lot of fun.  There are a lot of great options, choices, and a ton of wonderfully Star Wars models!  It's an excellent system, and the dice rolling is fairly random, but it usually ends up feeling more epic than frustrating.  I really do love this game.

RATING: 5
I am really impressed.

I played a demo in Essen and the game felt kinda...meh.

But now after I played the full game and three scenarios in a row I can say that FFG have learned a lot from Descent 2.0.

Now what is really better and what make IA (up for now) the best Overlord crawler to date - sadly, I do not enjoy them that much today.

1.) Hidden information - Rebels will be surprised in every mission and the old DOOM/Descent 1 tabletalk is back!

2.) Mission style are (up for now) really different. Defend a base, strike in to get secret plans without getting wounded, reache goals in a sepcific number of rounds [so not only rush missions here!!!)

3.) Nice combination between skills (rebels and Imperial player)

4.) Interesting campaign system with a good story focus.

5.) Theme - Descent 2.0 felt kinda flat for me but this game really rocks!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I like games that tell stories while also being mechanically interesting. Imperial Assault is such a game for me. The following only concerns the campaign mode and is mainly from the viewpoint of the "dungeon master" who controls the Galactic Empire.

In itÂ´s core, its a tactical shooter, meaning you have to position your guys tactically and strategically on the board. Selecting the order in which you activate your units and their positioning is crucial. The line-of-sight system allows you to sometimes block important figures from being shot at. Units are represented by miniatures that are fun to paint and I would highly recommend you do that.
In most scenarios there are some triggered events that happen either at fixed points in time or when the heroes do something specific. This maintains some level of surprise for the hero players and keeps the story going. The players quickly try to play around some potential bad situations like opening a door with the last action and being surprised by a squad of stormtroopers.
I think the units and their abilities enable a relatively deep tactic game although you also need some luck when rolling the dice. I donÂ´t mind the luck-factor since it also comes with many moments of tension/joy/small frustration that make a game engaging.
Most of the scenarios are close and come down to the last action in order to determine the winner. The possibility of upgrading your characters with new weapons and skills together with the continuing story motivates for the next game and keeps it interesting.

I would say I like the game for the same reasons I like X-Wing the Miniatures Game. Both are set in the same universe, both use dice as well as cards that represent units and describe their values and abilities, nice miniatures and a tactic movement aspect. The big differences are the number of players (2 vs. 2-5), the grid board vs. no grid, the turn order (sequentially vs. "simultaneously") and the fact that one tells a story from beginning to end.

RATING: 7
After playing through two full campaigns, I have decided this game just isn't for my group. Too often, the game suffered from bad die rolls; many times, people played tactfully, consulting with each other as a team, and then just got hosed on the rolls. I know that's the point of these games, but we just did not find it as fun as we hoped.

The game is dripping theme, though, and many of the missions felt the way they should from that perspective. The story quests, and the characters specific missions were tense and satisfying most of the time. It was the dull filler missions to get to the final battle that left us wanting. Not a bad game, just not for us. 

Willing to trade.

RATING: 9
Initial Impressions:
-----------------------
First off, the components for this one are amazing.  Everything just does an amazing job to say "I'm Star Wars" and it really makes the game feel thematic.  The scenario system is pretty cool, and makes each campaign unique, and this will improve as they add more character packs + big box expansions.  

The LOS is still not perfect, but its an improvement over Descent 2e.  I like that they removed stupid things like traps, but made the Imperial "building up" still interesting.  

The game is still a bit "Gamey" and in fact, the team on the clock so far in my games has always lost, with only one of them really being close.  Still need to play more, and read some FAQ stuff to make sure all this stuff was legal ( I am pretty sure it all was ).

I really enjoyed my plays, and even after playing all day, I wanted to keep going, however the system still has some issues.  I probably had too high of expectations, and while I still think X-Wing is the better Star Wars game, this one is still amazing, and I look forward to years of playing this one!

RATING: 8
The setup can take a while, but after half a dozen plays or so, you've probably worked out a good system so you know where everything is and can find it quickly.

I've mostly played the campaign as the Imperial player and this game came pretty naturally to me. I was able to crush the Rebellion with one swift stroke ... thus terminating my cousins' desires to play this with me. You have to be ready for some tactics if you play this game, and you have to STICK TO THE MISSION! Don't get side-tracked blasting stormtroopers. I, personally, find the run-and-gun type of mission much more evoking of the Star Wars theme than a more combat oriented game where the goal is to wipe out the opposing Imperial units.

The skirmish mode is nice to have. It's a fun little diversion, but having played WotC's Star Wars Miniatures game and currently owning Star Wars LCG and X-Wing, I think I have enough games that require customization before you play. What this game lacks in the options of the old Star Wars Miniatures games, this game makes up for in mechanics. I like that each skirmish game has random mission objectives so you are trying to juggle between wiping the other force out or getting mission objectives. It feels very innovative to me and I really like hat aspect. If I didn't have so many games like the skirmish mode, I think I'd be way more excited about it.

RATING: N/A
Paid $43 from Barnes and nobles during 50% sale. Play campaign a few times to see all core set missions!
Best add ons: Villains; Royal Guard Champion, Hired guns, Han or Chewie: wave 1- no expansions needed. Twin shadows expansion- adds mini campaign, 6 new missions, 4 new characters: tusken raiders/ heavy storm troopers, two heroes (Biv Bodrhik, Saska Teft). Later waves: Obi wan Kenobi. Ally/Villian packs add one mission each. Return to Hoth is a big-box full campaign add-on, better value than Twin Shadows.

When playing two person- one player controls 2, 3, 4 rebels.  If playing 2: each gets a "Legendary" Reward card. It adds +10 Health and both Heroes get an additional "activation token" which means they both get two turns in one round as compared to the Imperial Player's Deployment cards which get only one activation per round.  If playing 3: each gets a "Heroic" Reward card. It adds +3 to Health and 1 of the 3 Heroes (rotating) gets an additional "activation token" which means they basically get two turns in one round. Best balance is if you play 4 heroes though!

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Wow. Played two scenarios, and they were both supremely stupid. The figures and art look like Star Wars, but of course it feels like a mindless dungeon crawl because it's just Descent with a new coat of paint. Apparently FFG just got the Star Wars license, realized they need to crank out as many Star Wars games as humanly possible, and said "hey, we've got Descent on the shelf, we could turn it into a Star Wars game with almost no effort". Which is about what it feels like they put into it, at least on the game and scenario design end.

At least the scenarios are much shorter than Descent or Doom. They're dumb, but at least they're over reasonably quickly.

The game system is actually not too bad here. The dice are fine, the surges powering special abilities are decent. The recovery rules are broken, but what the hell. If there was more interesting terrain rules, more opportunity for maneuver, maybe more space to play with, and if the game had been given a non-stupid narrative arc, there might be something here. But sadly those things were not done. Think of some random terrible Star Wars tie-in novel.

At the end of the day I generally dislike FFG's games anyway, so YMMV. If you liked Descent for some reason and are a Star Wars fan, this might be your game :) But it's not mine.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
It is Star Wars Descent. All I ever needed this to be is Star Wars Descent.

I would say there are less differences between this game and the last iteration of the system (Descent 2e) compared to previous new editions (the Descent 1e > 2e jump; the Doom > Descent jump), and I feel confident in saying this is the definitive version.

First, they fixed what in my opinion is Descent 2e's only serious flaw: its broken-ass spawning system. The overlord (Imperial Player) cannot just respawn his biggest baddie the turn you kill it every single time and must once again generate threat (now using a handy wheel instead of tokens) in order to respawn troops.

Beyond fixing what was obviously broke, the next two most important changes, in my opinion, are that the overlord (Imperial player) now swaps turns with the heroes and that the maps are much smaller. These are two of the subtler changes, but they make all the difference.

The turn swap means less downtime generally and makes planning effectively much more difficult (and thus interesting) for both sides, who now have to react in real time. It brings out the psychological warfare of such games better - anticipating each sides moves, etc., is brought to the emotional forefront of the battle.

As for the smaller dungeons... the play space might look less epic on the surface, but really it just makes things feel tighter and more focused. Individual sessions now last the 90-120 minutes they ought, and it's rare that anyone goes a turn without doing anything important or lagging behind.

There's also some other smaller, but hugely welcome component changes that make setup go a good deal faster. No more fiddling about with terrain tiles - the colored lines on the map are more than sufficient (although I will point out that my red-green colorblind boyfriend had no end of trouble with the thin red lines).

The way scaling works for different player counts - beefing up the heroes rather than having a million different versions of each individual monster - also speeds things up and works even better.

The rulebook is also just straight up better. True to Fantasy Flight, we encountered some ambiguities here and there but most everything was covered and the general layout of the rulebooks is excellent. There's a handy index for absolutely everything so even clearing up confusion was a more pleasant experience compared to Imperial Assault's forebears.

The new side mission system is certainly interesting and exciting, although I'm not sure how I feel yet about upgrade paths being tied directly to characters. I'm not sure I'm the audience for that as I would prefer a little more customization. But that's a pretty minor and very personal complaint.

I am a huge Star Wars fan, though I do miss the fantasy theme and would love to see a Descent 3e. But I have to say, this is just an all-around better and cleaner experience out of the box than Descent 2e.

Update: Mostly done with our first campaign now and it's safe to say that this game IS pretty unbalanced, in the Rebel's favor. I understand that it's basically impossible to balance an asymmetric game 100%, but there were some missions (Friends of Old) where it seems like there's no way the Rebels could lose. If the game must be unbalanced, I would prefer that whichever side won the last mission (or at least, story mission) then receives a mission that's NOT in their favor for their next (story) mission.

I think this is in part due to the fact that the Rebels pretty much always have a unit (and thus turn) advantage. After the first turn or so, the Imperial player is usually stuck with 2-3 groups on the map to the Rebels' 4-5, giving them about half the number of turns each round, which can really hurt when there's no "delay" option for the Imperial player.

review from dad: When I was a teenager I found several dozen of the 80s Marvel Star Wars comics at a thrift shop in my hometown for 25c a piece and I bought them all. They were wacky, ridiculous, 'real house of ideas'/Marvel in it's glory days 80s supertrash. It sent me on a several year quest through my teens to collect every Star Wars comic Marvel had produced before the series untimely cancellation. I eventually succeeded, finding #1 for next to nothing a real coup.

Apologies for the aside it was just so I can say that playing IA is the closest I have felt to what my 12 year old self felt(yes 30 years ago) reading those old Marvel Star Wars mags. The excitement, the joy. Tonight the boy's character Mac got to drive an AT-ST. This game delivers on what it promises.

RATING: 6
I don't enjoy this game very much. It's for two primary reasons: (1) my son is so good at it, and I'm so mediocre, that it's a mismatch every time, like playing chess against a master when you're just a duffer, and this is somewhat further exacerbated by the campaign where you feel like you're having to play not just one game as a fun one-off, but instead a dozen missions of likely losing drudgery; and (2) the game is somewhat fiddly, forcing the player to keep a lot in his head as he's playing, and I don't do so well at this -- reminds me of other fiddly games like Sentinels of the Multiverse or World of Warcraft the Board Game, where you're constantly having to mentally adjust for +2's and -1's before and after comparing every roll. Not so fun -- better for computer games. I completely forgot about an ability for one of my two characters for half a match; on the one hand, thatâs on me to remember all of his several abilities, so I deserve to have missed it; on the other, the more abilities you add the more fiddly things get, the slower the play goes, and the more likely I am to make mistakes. The game lends itself to micromanagement as you progress. 

My son, meanwhile, is nailing the math so fast that he already knows what dice to root for on defense. I sit there trying to remember who has Pierce X, plus that Old Wounds means I do one less damage each roll, but that Savagery means with those guys he does one more each roll, etc. Maybe it's partially that I'm having to handle two full characters with two activations each (two player game, where I'm Rebel) -- maybe if I just had one character and was teamed with friends playing one character each, that would be more fun and less fiddly and unfunny.

It's a good design, of course. And my son is a great, great player -- I would love to see him compete against someone really good, as that would be a lot more competitive for him. As it is, though, it's hard for me to want to get this back to the table. I would prefer other wargames that are one-offs, not campaigns, that are more streamlined and less fiddly. There is also Skirmish mode. Maybe that would be more fun. 

Overall, I find myself much more interested in setting up a game with him of Battle Lore or Battle of the Five Armies, or Combat Commander. Or, also great, playing co-op with this talented young man who's always fun to join in with, rather than be beaten by! So for me, a 6 out of 10 based on what I've seen of this game. I had high(er) hopes.

Star Wars: Imperial Assault is my first foray into the Descent system and our first "date" was enjoyed by me. I was a little surprised that the game is quite light rulewise (with the exception of some timing questions). It's more like a beer and pretzels game than a serious brain burner. Chuck some dice and have fun. That's about it. The only thing one could hold against it was brought up by someone from my gaming group: "It's surprisingly unthematic for a Star Wars game". Although all the characters are there and you're fighting with the merchandise you'd expect in the Star Wars universe, we figured that it wasn't about epic battles but more like a race to achieve your objectives as fast as possible. Because of the harsh round limit in the first scenario, we didn't even bother to kill all the bad guys but raced for the terminals. In the end, we were swarmed by Storm Troopers but this didn't matter when we beat the last terminal to pieces.

RATING: 10
Terrific game. I've played through the Core Campaign, Twin Shadows and halfway through Hoth currently.
The Campaigns tend to vary in difficulty for the Rebels. Core isn't a cakewalk, but it is definitely the easiest campaign made yet. One issue with the campaign structure tends to be a rich-get-richer mechanic. If the Imperial wins he gets more xp, which puts him in a better position to win the next, etc. Snowballing in campaigns really is a risk I've found.
Skirmish, in my honest opinion, is the most balanced way to play this game and I'm very fond of the strategic side of it. Campaigns are fun stories, skirmish is all tactics.
Balancing can be a problem in some missions, and AP is certainly an issue with some groups, but neither sway me from loving this game.

RATING: 7.25
Imperial Assault is basically Descent 2.5 ed, but that's a good platform to work from, so why not?  There's the usual spate of changes, most of which are improvements.
Your heroes come in the usual archetypes (blaster-wielding smuggler, jedi-esque woman with staff, veteran trooper, grizzled commander, etc.), and are locked into their classes.  (Unlike in 2nd, where you can select your sub-class from a menu of choices.)  This gives you starting equipment and a series of purchasable abilities, just like 2nd.  You move and act exactly as before.  The only change is the dice, where they've reconfigured how attack and defense works.  It's a minor thing, but you no longer have that 1/6th miss chance on your own attack dice.  Instead, it's a 1/6th chance to dodge on the white dice (only used when attacking agile enemies; most use the black die).  This is less frustrating.
Also patched is the way that heroes heal and are KO'ed.  It's always been that if you were short stamina (now 'strain') for some effect, it down-converted to health loss.   But the other way was never true until now.  Now, if you've got 3 strain on your sheet and you have a capacity of 4, you can rest and get all 3 off, plus heal one HP.  If you do run out of HP, you flip over to your wounded side (which has one fewer base ability & some slightly lowered stats) and go back to full health.  The OL has to kill you [i]again[/i] to knock you out of the game.  We've yet to see this happen, since healing is so much easier.  (Rest twice on your turn and that'll put you back near full if you had no strain used).  This means there's a little less of an incentive to just try and murder the weakest character (unless the mission requires you to).
Threat is back, and spawning enemies is back too.  It's not near as annoying as 1st ed, though, since reinforcements can only be brought in through specific spawn points.
The scenario design is about the same as 2nd ed, for all the good and bad that implies.  There are some small improvements, though, with decisions that the OL player gets to make when he has a chance to throw a wrench in things.  The campaign is also reworked, with more branching paths and better incorporation of side mission choices.  
I've had fun with it so far, and we've been holding on, despite the number of times where it seems like the scenario is hopelessly stacked against us.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Have only done partial play/walthroughs. Need to play a full skirmish and campaign.

RATING: 7.33

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Rating is based on campaign mode only. I had to go with a 3 as it best describes how I feel... that is, not likely to play it again, but maybe in a year or so I could be convinced to play a shorter campaign, with only 1 or 2 Rebel players.

The 1-vs-n format of the game creates serious problems for competitive play and it was common to go for 2-3 hours with my character only have 6-8 meaningful actions. I would contribute to other Rebel turns, but to avoid being that "alpha" player I didn't try to force my opinion on others.  Further, if other Rebels made decisions I didn't agree with and I couldn't convince them otherwise I would feel frustration towards them... not good (I haven't felt this way in other co-op games... probably because you're playing against the game, not an opponent). I just can't reconcile a game where 1 of 5 players takes up over half of the game play time and has the opportunity to make 5x more meaningful decisions than other players. Additionally, the missions take too long in general, the round limit forces the Imperial player to use tactics that are not fun to play against (i.e. blocking, stunning, delaying, beating us down while the Rebels are effectively running away, etc.) and the hidden information is annoying (an AT-ST appears out of nowhere, Imperial characters next to a crate recover damage, etc.). There's not much story to the campaign either... a few sentences before each mission and a few sentences at the end, in fact, I'm not sure what the story was and I was never able to feel any attachment to my character, which may have been because our plays were spread out.

I also feel this is a difficult game to play competitively as the Imperial... again, the tactics you need to use are frustrating and you'll take heat from the Rebels. Also, since the Imperial does consume so much of the game play, you're likely to feel rushed as you don't want everyone else waiting on you throughout the game. Further, the Imperial player has to invest even more time than the Rebels to prepare the missions and such.

I'm glad we tried this but I just can't see investing another 30-40 hours on this game at this point... I would much rather get repeated plays of deeper strategy games like Tzolkin or Caverna.

It's a shame because I really wanted to like this game... the components are great, the IP is great and combat solid, just not for me. The 2 player skirmish mode may be a better fit and would likely have a much higher rating.

RATING: 5
These are not the droids you're looking for.

I really believe that many of the high ratings here are due to the convergence of Star Wars fandom and Descent. Which is exactly why I spent months like a kid before Christmas waiting for this game to come out.

Boy have I been dissapointed.

This is not simply Descent in the Star Wars universe as many other commenters have said. Although the basic mechanic is the same, the "minor" differences create a dramatically different game play experience.

Alternating turns means that not only do turns grind on, there is much less collaboration between the rebels. Unlike Descent where my group carefully plots each turn together, in Imperial Assault, we take our turns individually for the most part and then play Tetris on our phones while waiting for our turn to come around again.

The "narrative" function of each encounter means that the Imperial player must plan carefully and well in advance in order to have a coherent strategy throughout the encounter.

The game drags on and on, really. My group has been playing Descent almost weekly for two years and it's rare that an encounter takes more than 3 or 4 hours. But 4 hours seems to be the minimum time to get through an Imperial Assault encounter, which means it's not really appropriate for our regular after work Tuesday game night.

Finally, my biggest gripe with the game is that it really does not do justice to the flavor of Star Wars universe. Chewbacca never hid from storm troopers until he could find a med pack. And yet the best way for the rebels to win in this game is to proceed cautiously and methodically. The objective for the Imperial player is usually to inflict enough wounds that the heroes are considered "wounded". This causes the heroes to spend a lot of effort to avoid becoming wounded rather than heroically pursuing their goals.

So, my group which is Star Wars and Descent obsessed, has abandoned our campaign after about 7 encounters. You cant say we didn't give it the college try. But in the end, we were tired of not enjoying our weekly game night.

RATING: 6
I have a complicated, love/hate relationship with the Descent and SW:IA model. Itâs only been recently that I think Iâve come to understand that this game system is not what I ultimately want it to be, whether I play it 1vMany or coop. Iâm expecting a more strategic experience when the dice overpower any efforts at good play. Whatâs more, I come for a dungeon crawl only to learn itâs a dungeon race.

My group and I played a yearlong Descent v1 campaign using Road to Legend. I went from loving the game to loathing it. The big problem with Descent v1 was how the Overlord would activate all his guys at once and pick off one guy (often strolling by others; [i]ta ta![/i]) until he was knocked out. Then repeat. This was THE way for the OL to play every scenario as it worked. And the downtime waiting for him to maximize his 10+ dudes and cards was annoying. It felt imbalanced in terms of both power and fun. Throw in lots of rules ambiguities and the fun got sucked out, and we simply ended things prematurely because nobody was having fun.

Then SW:IA comes along and, as wary as I am, I'm convinced to try it -- and lo and behold, I find it fixes just about everything I hated about Descent. The one-at-a-time monster group activation vs all-at-once is brilliant so turns go much more quickly and things feel much more balanced (no more monster parade of suffering 12 attacks before your next turn). We meet monthly for a 1vMany campaign. Iâm in love again! 

But something is bothering me about these games. I leave my sessions fried after 4+ hours of parsing out permutations of movements and actions to find the best one because the result always comes down to the dice anyway. I donât feel that the player agency is commensurate with the effort required. You can figure out the best order of moves all you want, but the dice will determine the story told. 

I originally rated the game a 10, but after playing the campaign once through, Iâve found the balance is nowhere near what Iâd like it be. Weâre all strong players but have found unless the Imperial Player doesnât play well, the odds are stacked grossly in his favor due to how much regeneration and surprise he has on his side. Thatâs because the game effectively cheats in order to balance things. You get done wiping out some enemy figures in your way but â surprise â a new group spawns right where they were or even behind you. 

Frequently, hours of careful tactical moves on our part are undone by spawns and events that we could not have foreseen. Again, I get the concept behind that, but it often renders hours of careful planning moot because â surprise spawn! I long for the control in Descent whereby players could control where monsters spawned. 

In one session, we rolled dodges on the first 7 of 8 rolls and got off to a fast start but that still wasnât enough to stop the spawn-athon that happened near our end goal. We just couldnât fight through it all and made the scenario feel unwinnable. I just wish there was either an AI that adjusted as the game went on (for example, only so many figures allowed on the board at a time) or the players have one "No Spawn" card they can play once a game when it spins out of control against them. Unfortunately the game is frequently punishing and over before itâs over.

I also question the choice to have the game become quality vs quality AND quantity. Shouldn't the rebels be markedly stronger than the enemies? [see Conan for how that's done well] Instead it's a game of equals vs equals plus numbers. Our Imperial Player astutely bought the power whereby when we rested, he could make an interrupt attack or movement which really hampered our ability to recover. Just brutal. That needs to be nerfed. 

In the end, I've come to realize that I'm not very good at this game because I approach it as a dungeon crawl when it's more of a run-n-gun to reach the next objective point. Which is fine, but it's not what I want when I sit down to play this type of game.

RATING: 9
Very strategic and tense tactical combat, a genuine Star Wars feel, diverse characters with interesting leveling options, and a ton of great components. Although somewhat disconnected, the branching campaign sets the replayability through the roof, as it adds to the map modularity and the multiple play modes. The game is optimized for campaign play, so it almost requires playing with a consistent group. I would have appreciated an easy way to jump into scenarios with rotating players: it is still possible to manage (as we have attempted to), but keeping track of the level, resources, and chosen paths lengthens the already busy inter-mission bookkeeping. Perhaps other similar games (like TMNT or Conan) would better serve such a play approach.

The biggest downside for me is that the imperial player alternates between rooting for herself (you want to deploy your best tactics) and rooting for the hero team (you want everyone to have a good time). No victory (imperial or rebel) feels complete when you are alone. :( Therefore, I really look forward for the release of the delayed app to transform this into a fully-cooperative adventure, and hope that the tactical depth does not suffer in the process. Such an app would greatly improve the already awesome experience that is this huge game.

RATING: 10
The most epic campaign style game to date.

The SW theme really allows you to immerse yourself and the scripted events in each mission add even more flavor.

This game is very tactical in nature, more so than descent. This can be a bit brutal for the rebels, who are usually out gunned and aren't aware of the possible events in each mission.

If you need to win every mission, this game is not balanced for that as certain missions are harder for one side or another and dice rolls play a big factor. If you take it as a story game all leading up to the finale, you will really enjoy the experience.

For a more balanced tactical game, the skirmish mode is excellent and FFG is doing a good job of promoting it in stores. It plays fast and is very tactical with lots of options

RATING: 7.5
It's a pretty good Descent reskin.  Feels very thematic.  Even though it has some problems and the scenarios drag a little in the middle, it always ends with me wanting to play another mission.  

Does a decent job providing some RPG elements that make me feel invested in my character.  

So far in the campaign I am playing it feels a bit too easy for the heroes but I think it would be a different story if the DM imposed a time limit on our talking strategy and he's pretty lenient when we are like "oh I meant to go here."  

It also potentially suffers the alpha gamer problem (depending on the group).

With some of the recent games that have come with this style, either all v 1 or all v the box, you would have to be a star wars fan to pick this above the rest.  Fortunately, it really feels like star wars - even to the point of the AT-ST feeling a little silly, unwieldy and large for what it actually accomplishes, just like in the movies!

Seriously, the AT-ST has to cupid shuffle it's way around the map.

Oh and the characters offer a nice amount of diversity and synergy.  I would never to choose to play with anything less than a full complement of characters.

RATING: 5.7
I would love to rate it a 9,  but I can't it just doesn't feels thematic enough.  That Imperium seems to have infinite resources but actually the Imperial player just brings one stormtrooper Per round in, but heroes will kill up to three per round. Tanks like Gaarkhan can ignore good positioning, he just rushes through it all  and is almost impossible to kill (rushing strategy works in most of my played missions, so where are tactical decisions?). In Descent the overlord has some action cards to spice up the course of the campaign, in imperial assault those cards are moved in the skirmish game.  So playing as the heroes is fun, playing as the imperial player feels very limited. Rarely buying one command card is not enough. You wanna field an elite unit, forget it, much too expensive. You can't wait 4 turns to save up points for it or rebels will have finished their goals already. Agenda cards are mostly useless too (f.i. Add Sidemission xyz to the campaign -> why should I care about this?). A lot of wasted potential.  Furthermore it is almost impossible to bring those expansionpack Minis like Vader or Solo into campaign play. It seems that this game Is much more developed for skirmish mode instead of campaign, which is just clunky.

Edit: The more missions I play, the more I have to downrate this game. Nearly broken Mission "12 o'clock p.m." Page 8. Setup time 20min, play time 5min. Guys, this mission is rubbish. Granted it could work with 4 heroes, but with 3 = one with double activation token it just did not.  The player who controls Jyn gets the second activation token and rushes to the comlink.  With a successful test the door opens and she can shoot a first time on Szark. With 2nd Action a 2nd Shot. Then immediately again 2 Attacks (2nd act. token) and Szark is dead before the Imperial player could do one action. Game over. Now judge yourself if this is broken or not.
 I will continue to downrate this game with every mission that is a disgrace in balancing.

This game needs an App so bad!

RATING: 7
Being an adult gamer with a wife but no life-or-death responsibilities to rear children is a fraught thing. It reminds one that to many, living a biologically unreproductive life, a life devoted to life itself, learning, art, fun, and other indulgences--is ultimately, to a certain, distinctly puritan frame of thinking, selfish. When we are finally too old to fully enjoy those things, we will only have each other, or the crumbling infrastructure of the state, to care for our needs. It is potentially frightening enough that one chooses not to consider it at all, but to defer that anxiety elsewhere.

Playing Descent, in either edition, was a hatefully stupid thing. It required you to think long and hard about decisions which were ultimately out of your control anyway. It allowed for zero creative engagement with the game, instead plugging any question you might have about the way your characters interacted with the world with a card with plus-one something printed on it. But I liked the figurines, and in quiet hours I learned to paint them pretty well. Nonetheless, I have never met a human being who found the experience of playing Descent more fun or interesting than a thousand similar alternatives. And I kept trying to make the game good, with house rules and various incentives. This never worked, but it did became a small obsession, indicative of a greater anxiety about things not in the game, anxieties about one's place in the world, about the idea that when one is dead, one may go childless and unremembered in the larger weave of the world.

Imperial Assault is a mildly improved version of the Descent system. It is just as satisfying to paint. My friends will play it (as long as I keep certain needlessly complex elements of the game structure hidden), because, for them, as for myself, Star Wars is an unadulterated source of nostalgia. I own everything for the system, and sometimes dote on the game in a way that puzzles and vexes me, because it does not reward extended thought. And one day, hopefully a long time from now, my lifeless body will be reduced to carbon in an unfurnished room with an antiseptic odor to it, and I will no longer have any anxieties or obsessions to worry about.

RATING: 9.1
The only reason It gets this high rating is because of Skirmish. The replay value and the customization options are the most interesting parts about this game for me. 
Campaign mode is also fun, but bot as deep as you would like. 

I like all games, but one of my favorites is Magic, and i can relate to this kind of games that gives you the chance to customize and build your own "deck" "army"  or what ever to battle against other players. Now, add the Star wars theme to it, and it becomes even more interesting for me.

The biggest concern for me is if It stays long enough active to invest the time and money on it. So far they have done an excellent job at having a healthy environment in the Skirmish mode and keeping fairly everything playable and somewhat balanced. So, the longer this game stays around, the better it gets if they keep printing content that fixes those early mistakes...things like cost curve, OP stats or abilities, and other interactions that could smooth the game. Also, once the game feels in a good spot, They could start bringing new elements to the game that could potentially deepen the game play.
Basically is a great game for what i was published for (campaign being little behind) and with great potential to become even bigger...Not as Magic, but as x-wing.

Finally, as a positive point for those who like the campaign mode more, FFG has announced that they are working on an app that could definitely could change deeply the dynamic of the game, even said to have a solo option...We will see how good or bad this app can be.

Enjoyed the games for as long as i had it, but at the end i wasn't play it much since i moved away from my playing group.so traded it. i also updated my rating.

RATING: 10
Imperial Assault is the best miniatures game I have ever played. It is incredibly thematic and entertaining. The developers at FFG have done an amazing job of making you feel like you are actually taking part in a Star Wars story line and all of the missions play out like a part from the movie or an episode of Rebels.

The quality of all the components is very high, especially compared to other games. The minis are a blast to paint and really come to life on the board. The combat is very well thought out and engaging with their own unique die that pertain to each individual character's abilities. For instance, red die are very high damage, green die have good damage with range utility, blue are high range but low utility, white defense die are for agile characters while black are for armored etc.. it just all fits very, very well.

The price point is high but you also get 2 games in one with the skirmish. Skirmish is great even if you just want to play head to head with friends, or you can take it a step forward and join the great online community. But even if you never plan to play skirmish, the campaign itself is very lengthy with a lot of options. 11 missions will take your roughly 20-25 hours to complete, then you can play again and see very few of the same missions depending on if you win or lose. Then as you add blister packs you get more missions which expands the choices even further.

I could go on forever about this game, the bottom line is that balance wise it is great, quality wise it is great and entertainment wise you are getting all you pay for and more.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.75
This game has had its ups and down's with me. I had considered trading it until the announcement of the App that would run the Imperial side of the game. This made me excited to keep the game and see how the app would go. 

Since the release of the App, I have had fun with the game again. I have started to try to pick up all big box and character expansions. 

Before App: 6.5
After App: 7.75

RATING: 8.5
As A Star Wars fan, I was looking for a dungeon crawler/dice game so this was an obvious one. a friend of mine has Descent 2nd and I like it so this seems like a good match.

Campaign mode seems a bit automatic but still has progression. Some mission a for sure balance for one faction of the other. The success of these mission are only worth their reward in progression. Each side wants to win to get stronger so you get ready for the final mission. It's done well but the campaign does simulate one thing right based on the movies. The Rebels are outgun and it's in their best interest to get on the objective as quickly as possible. This is not a campaign where you can just try to kill everyone to win. If you try this, you will lose. There is always a timing going on so the Rebels are best to achieve their goals as quickly as possible and leave. Hit and run tactics is very similar to what the rebels have to do in the movies.

If it was only for the campaign, this game would still be great but the huge advantage this game has over its predecessor (descent) is the inclusion of a skirmish mode. Now, if you can't get your group going to continue to campaign, you can play a 2 player game and people get to "build" their army to their licking and power. This brings this game over its predecessor and open it up to more than just a dungeon crawler.

lo tiene casi todo pintado, por cierto. :D (esta QTCGS)

Descent engine aplicado a La Guerra de las Galaxias. No hace falta decir nada mas. 

Todo lo bueno y todo lo malo de Descent (con algunas correcciones sobre la materia) aplicado a la trama argumental mas reconocible de los ultimos casi 50 aÃ±os. 

Para mi gusto el defecto mas reseÃ±able (ya sabeis que se me da mejor seÃ±alar los defectos que las virtudes xD) es que, dentro de la tematica, el argumento y elementos incluidos son muy de universo expandido. del universo expandido nuevo hecho acordemente con disney, se entiende, ya que el antiguo UE ha sido declarado obsoleto. 

Me refiero por ejemplo a que los personajes jugadores son todos inventados y practicamente irreconocibles en las peliculas u otros materiales expandidos (quiza sean personajes de los actuales juego de rol o del LCG, pero como no tengo el placer con ninguno de los dos...). ocurre lo mismo con el desarrollo de misiones, los enemigos... y eso en mi opinion le resta un punto en inmersividad. 

Los personajes conocidos (luke, han solo, chuwi, leia, darth vader, boba fett, etc) entran al juego como "lugartenientes" que pueden participar con o contra los jugadores siguiendo determinadas reglas del escenario. Esto lo hace muy inmersivo, pero mucho menos que manejar a los propios protagonistas. Una "operacion desperre" que se veia venir de lejos, en toda su maxima expresion. 

Jugablemente y en calidades todo aquello que se puede decir de descent es aplicable a IA. 

pero en mi opinion de momento IA no es el amerigame definitivo. es un excelente juego, pero va a estar demasiado monetizado y se han mojado demasiado poco en los contenidos para que en mi valoracion llegue a ser absolutamente magnifico. 

pd. ni me molesto en llevar la cuenta de las expansiones que ha comprado yera de este juego. escribo "todas" y me juego las uÃ±as de los meÃ±iques de los pies a que acierto. xD

RATING: 2
Repetitive.  The intellectual property (it is better than Descent because it is Star Wars skin/theme) is the only reason this game is ranked so highly.  If you don't believe me but are curious as to whether I am right: good for you.  This uses the same mechanics seen in Descent, Descent 2, Gears of War, Doom, and the list continues.  Fantasy Flight makes tweaks here and there and changes the game into more of a campaign mode than earlier games.  Bottom line - the game is the same.  You could write the "progression" of players into quests in Descent without campaign mode.  Regardless that isn't the most important point.

The combat is repetitive, it doesn't matter if it is Darth Vader, an AT-ST, E-Web, or storm trooper.  The number of dice are different and the variety a bit, but it is the same combat over and over and over.  The game is almost exclusively about combat with interludes that are illusions of non-combat.  Kill this person, kill these terminals, area control these zones (you control by killing people).

The game isn't strategy, it is tactics.  Even that is being generous.  The white die is the worst die in the game.  It goes from block 0 damage to block infinite damage (ALL).  Realistically that means you could take 8 wounds or 0 wounds (1/6 chance of either event.  That spread makes luck a dominant factor.  The black defense die is the best, it goes from 0 to 3 as the spread of how much it blocks.  I am too much in the details, but the game is basically a poor version of craps.

It is beautiful, if I rated a game on beauty and how great star wars is then I would give this game a 9 or 10.  Problem is it is a game and not a dollhouse.  It makes a great dress up dolls experience, but not a good or even OK board game.

RATING: 9
Sold
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I've played the first mission twice (trying to find a group to play a campaign with). It is an excellent game and seems to improve on Descent in many ways. Keeping the mission objectives secret makes the game unique (I'm not sure if Descent had that rule too, but if it did we didn't play it like that). The game's mechanics are simply tools to accomplish a thematic objective. I can't think of another game that accomplishes this. Some players dislike the hidden objective rule, but I find it very thematic. Plus, if it's anything like Descent, knowing the objective ahead of time would almost make the game boring. 

The threat system makes the Imperial player (which I played once) feel like they have real choices to make. I think this is an improvement over Descent's Overlord Card system, since the Rebel players can account for the increasing threat and aren't randomly caught off guard by certain events. However, our normal Overlord/Emperor seems to prefer the Overlord card system (he's never played the good guys, so that might account for it). 

The components, as always, are beautiful. I have painted all of the core minis (pictures are on by BGG user page gallery) and had a wonderful time doing it. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Core set has incredible value. Truly a FFG core set game that requires no expansions for the full experience.

Streamlined dungeon crawler design. Just the right amount of rules to keep round-to-round admin minimal and not get in the way of action. Essentially you don't get the feeling that a computer should be doing all this math.

Finally the most dynamic sculpts of any miniatures game. In many other games, the miniatures are posing rather than striking. In Imperial Assault, all the sculpts are in the midst of performing an action lending to the imagination of a chaotic fire-fight.

RATING: 6
In a nutshell, this game is flawed, but fun. If you can look past the rules oddities (easier to do if you're a die-hard SW fan) and the way the gameplay can swing wildly in favor of one side or the other, you might enjoy this. Among the many complaints: Heroes can run through or past formations of stormtroopers with impunity; they get hit with blaster fire 20+ times and are only "wounded" (not that you can tell); they CAN'T die or be captured no matter what happens. This results in some crazy tactical decisions since there's really nothing to fear by running into a field of overwhelming firepower. Clearly, there are "game reasons" for these rules, but they do not fit the theme. Not a game to be taken too seriously. Looks like it's a big hit, so FFG will get rich selling unpainted plastic minis at ten bucks a pop. (Seriously?)

RATING: 9
I love Star Wars. Ok, now that we got that out of the way...

I love board games! 

I love character building! And story! And tactical movement/combat! And puzzle solving! And miniatures!

This game is essentially a Star Wars rpg-lite boardgame, and it does a fantastic job of realizing that experience. I think it's great the way it blends elements of character building and story from rpgs into a mechanically tight system that works for a board game. The way the campaign is structured into missions and side missions, with a branching story, gives the game a narrative breadth that eclipses most board gaming experiences, while the mechanics of the game are fun in their own right. It's exciting to play, and to not know what might be around the corner, or behind that door, and winning or losing the game really comes down to making good choices at key moments. 

It also does a lovely job at creating that Star Wars feel and atmosphere as well, which goes a long way in garnering my appreciation for the game. It's a lot of fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Initial rating one play only

I wasn't big on trying this at first knowing it was just a Descent re-skin (turns out it feels like a mix of Earth Reborn & Descent and almost more like the former), but it is SW afterall, and I wanted to see for myself if enough theme comes through to justify a purchase or not, short answer: still unsure

So I liked the mechanics for the most part which was not a surprise having played Descent in the past, and this version is pretty streamlined for FFG but of course it is the 3rd or 4th version so you would have hoped they would get it right (well, the first time would be nice really and without need of expansions to fix...) but there are a few questionable rules/tactics there too so its still not perfect but pretty good. Apparently most of the missions can be played in 2 or 3 hours, I like this and combined with the streamlining and only 13 pages of rules it should make it fairly easy to table. The biggest plus for me though was the game was damn fun [i]and[/i] had a nice bunch of decisions to make too, and not just campaign based, actual in-game decisions became interesting and damn tense at times too. I thought the Rules reference book was a great addition to FFG games, unfortunately it was not quite as comprehensive as it should have been so still need a really good FAQ and there is still some cross-referencing as a result too so a PITA there, but it was great to be able to really easily and quickly find most stuff, hopefully they will work on perfecting this in future because itâs a great concept. 

The scenario I played 2 player was just the intro one but was pretty neat, the imperial player had some tricks up his sleeve (like a DM where only he knows like Descent) that is sure to catch the Rebels off guard and make their life interesting and more challenging at the same time. Also great here is the time element, as the rebel player I was really under the gun to keep moving, without this the tension would have been way way less so this was a great addition here imo. At the end of the scenario I had to race into the final room, bruised and bloodied and out of time completely and my mission all came down to one dice roll so it felt very polished/balanced and damn tense and fun overall.  

From what I know the Campaign seems interesting enough, and this is almost always a nice draw/addition in a game where you have some choices on how to upgrade/shape your character for future missions etc. So the big question left for me was does the theme really come out enough in the game? To me, so far it feels like a combined crawl/tactical minis game with all the theme applied through text, unlike something like Queens Gambit where it really âfeelsâ like a star wars game (and not a dungeon crawl). The thing is QG only had one mission, but itâs a damn thematic good one (game has too much luck though). Here we have many missions, and even a campaign which is great but are they really star wars based? Well first mission seemed pretty generic, and apparently none of them play out scenes from the movies which to me is disappointing. I mean if we could try to rescue Solo from Jabba, now [i]this[/i] would feel thematic, and sure fans can come up with this stuff but why do we even have to go this route, jeepers. Yes you do get to "somewhat" use some of the big characters here (not as your main guy though) and you are playing in the SW universe I guess (text read aloud revolves around it) but I think much more could have been done to bring the theme out. 

So to sum up, by far the Best SW "boardgame" (not counting pure "ship" or card games) to date with no close competitor (QG & Assault on Hoth runners up but both too simple & too much luck), but I hope they do something original and much more thematic with the universe, we need more simulation otherwise it just feels tacked on with a bunch of flavor text. As I somewhat mentioned it really does almost remind me more of Earth Reborn (tactical minis game) than Descent, but it definitely feels like both to me anyway and still retains some crawl feel. No idea on skirmish game myself (apparently good) but not that interested either, the campaign game is what I want to play if playing. Likely remain between 7 and 9 for me depending on richness of campaign and if I see more theme integration (not just a bunch of text), would have been easy 9 for me with more theme. As usual for me this is a 2 player only game, but I think will work very well for 3 players too and would happily play it 3. If I didnât own Earth Reborn I would jump on this, now the decision is a little harder since my shelf space is so tight, and they do scratch a VERY similar itch.

RATING: 5.5
2015-01-09

Initial Rating: 7.0 (January 2015)

I really like Descent (Second Edition) and as Imperial Assault is very Descent derivative I was sure I was going to love the game (right now I like it, but it has a lot of potential). Besides Star Wars is my favorite theme EVER so buying the game was a no brainer for me.

When I got my game, I was surprised to see a box so big. The game was much more expensive than I initially thought, but the game components are truly great, the miniatures are awesome and I have never seen a game that came with four different game manuals. Great job on the game component FFG.

So far I have only played the tutorial mission twice but it was very simple for us (and very difficult for the rebel payers), Iâm still glad it is there so it is easy to explain the game to new gamers, but if you are a seasoned Descent player you could perhaps begin with the campaign mode right away. The tutorial mission is also great because you can play the game after 30 minutes of unpacking it.

After playing it I noticed some differences between Imperial Assault and Descent, most prominent the heroes now can die (be defeated) and be out of the current scenarios, this is handled a la Earth Reborn in which the players get hurt first and they lose some abilities and if you hurt them again they are out. I really like this because it makes more thematic sense. Also all the minis can now move twice. 
 
Iâm also excited to see that the game came with a second mode of play (Skirmish) only for two players. I have to play it soon. 

Bottom line, Iâm sure that when I play the campaign mode (and the skirmish mode) the rating will go up. Iâm also sure this will be a huge money pit but I donât really care about that. On the other hand, Iâm not sure if I will keep it as I bought around 15 copies of the game for my friends and after I have played a campaign I usually donât have any desire to replay it. Perhaps the Skirmish mode can save the game for me, or perhaps I will keep it because it is Star Wars. We will see. I think Imperial Assaultis the best Star Wars game currently in print (I havenât played some of the OOP games), miles better than X-wing and better than the Card Game. 

According to my 2015 game commitments I need to play more campaign games so I think Imperial Assault will be it.

Current Rating: 7.0

RATING: 9
Rebel players use their character's abilities to try and defeat the one playing the Empire, either in one-on-one skirmishes or groups of players in scenarios (which in multiples become a campaign), using dice-based combat. Base box is expensive but comes with a lot: a variety of terrain, cards for each character to guide play, six hero minis, many more Imperials -- including an AT-ST! -- plus Luke and Vader figure packs, which show you the value of getting them beyond just the minis (scenarios too). However, it also comes with FOUR manuals, and you will find yourself returning to the reference manual which explains all the key terms in better detail than the introductory manuals.

PLUS: large variety of options in the base set, scenarios are really challenging
MINUS: cost

EXTRA PLUS: further figure sets mean that the game will have a long future of options

RATING: 7
I hated Descent 2.0, and this improves on Descent in practically every way despite being adapted from the same system.

Allowing movement through enemies changes the game from "quick, run to the goal before we get blocked by monsters" (Descent) to a tactical skirmish with interesting choices (Imperial Assault).

That goes in hand with another big change to the mission design. In Descent, almost every quest is a race, and the overlord player often has a divergent goal. The teams don't even really care what the other side is doing as long as they're doing it quicker. In Imperial Assault the Imperial player is fighting directly against the Rebels in almost every mission, with the win condition being "wound all four Rebel players".

Speaking of wounds, Descent's hero death is a joke. Heroes can spring back up on the next turn with no penalty. In Imperial Assault, not only is Rebel death part of the victory condition for the Imperial side, but being wounded actually changes the stats and abilities of the Rebel players! It's a thematic and dramatic element that Descent sorely lacks.

Imperial Assault adds more drama and tactics by allowing the Rebel and Imperial players to alternate turns. In Descent, all the heroes take their turns before the overlord even gets a chance to do anything. This often results in anticlimactic situations where the overlord summons a fantastic creature (e.g. master Shadow Dragon) only to watch it be slain before its first action. In Imperial Assault that rarely happens, and the Rebel players have to consider which enemy groups have acted when deciding who to target next.

The heroes killed a master Shadow Dragon, they should at least get some pleasure out of killing such a large beast, right? No, in Descent, the reinforcement rules often mean the overlord can simply revive the monster on the next turn. This further encourages the heroes to run for the goal and ignore the overlord. In Imperial Assault, the reinforcements are chosen by the Imperial player and arrive in groups so the Rebel players never know what to expect.

Further to that point, the campaign book in Imperial Assault is private information. Only the Imperial player knows what's coming. This again adds drama and tension to the mission.

Descent, with some rules re-writing, quest re-balancing and player co-operation, could be a good game, but I'm not willing to put the time and effort required to make it so, especially when Imperial Assault does everything better.

SUMMARY

DESCENT:

* Hero death is insignificant; they spring back up on the next turn
* Monsters can be wiped out before the Overlord even gets to use them
* Quests are unbalanced - it always feels like one side had no chance to win
* Quests are usually a race between the Heroes and the Overlord
* The Overlord's quest goal is often unrelated to the heroes, so both sides tend to avoid each other
* Reinforcements are predictable (usually one per turn) and weak
* Overlord card effects feel weak and insignificant
* Overlord cards are difficult to remember (there are 15 cards in the deck)
* Quest information is public, encouraging players to game the system instead of role-play
* Monsters completely block hero movement
* Line of sight is convoluted
* Figures can miss an attack (1 in 6 chance), which is anticlimactic
* Movement around corners when a monster is present feels unfair
* Large monster movement ("shrinking") feels silly and unfair
* Fatigue-movement feels unfair
* Heroes choose the quest order, so there's no chance for a linear story progression
* There isn't much story to follow anyway - the flavor text for each quest is sparse
* Travel is unrewarding and nearly flavorless

IMPERIAL ASSAULT:

* Rebels first become wounded (with reduced stats) and then withdraw from the mission entirely if they "die" a second time
* The Imperial player activates one group of enemies after each Rebel player
* Missions are incredibly balanced, often being decided in the final turn
* Quests have a bit more variety, and you are often racing a clock instead of each other
* The Imperial goal is usually just to stop the Rebels (all four Rebel players wounded)
* Reinforcements arrive in groups purchased (with threat points) by the Imperial player
* Agenda cards are purchased by the Imperial player and have stronger effects
* The Imperial player starts with 0 cards, so they are learned by all as the campaign unfolds
* Only the Imperial player sees the campaign book
* Enemy figures slow player movement, but never block it completely
* Line of sight is improved (although can still be frustrating if the players don't all understand them)
* Certain (nimble) figures can dodge an attack (1 in 6 chance), which is a bit more exciting and happens less often
* The movement around corners problem still exists, but doesn't come up as often because of how the maps are laid out, and because the Rebel players are usually not trying to avoid the monsters to race to the finish line
* Updated movement rules for large monsters make a bit more sense (no more "shrinking")
* Strain-movement is limited to two spaces per turn.
* Strain is also a more precious resource than fatigue due to how tight the mission balance is
* The Rebel players still choose the next mission, but the choice is more interesting for both sides
* It's still hard to follow the story, but each mission has decent flavor
* No travel

RATING: 4
Played one campaign where the Imperials ran away with the game.  Overall, it felt too much like a D&D dungeon crawler and not enough like Star Wars.  Having heroes who can absorb half a dozen blaster bolts (and who get better by crouching behind an object and shedding some strawberry jam) just didn't feel Star Wars-ish to me, and I didn't like a game system where most attacks hit.

Edit: After playing the Twin Shadows mini-campaign, I dropped it two more points.  The 8 round limit used in most scenarios feels unnecessary given the Imperial players' unlimited reinforcements and the game is too much about playing 'stupid' and running pell mell towards the objectives for me to enjoy it.  Balance also feels like hot garbage as the Imperials ran away with both campaigns despite early Rebel victories.

Rules are the latest variation of FFG's house system (Doom/Descent/Mutant Chronicles) - I think I preferred the MC incarnation, which put a higher premium on attack accuracy.  A lot of the interactions among cards are sloppy and not that well defined, which was a surprise given how often FFG has already gone to this well.

RATING: 7.5
Amazing campaign mode that engages the characters with their own personal missions (eventually) to make for a (mostly) narrative storyline. Much-streamlined version of the Descent system. 2 games in one box with Skirmish mode. Great production values overall (natch, it's FFG).

However, this doesn't prevent the game from becoming rather clunky and slow. Even small missions are taking my group 4 hours to complete as the heroes try to optimize their way through the puzzle that is a mission. Fiddly mission rules can be easily forgotten by Heroes and lead to "tainted" missions. Imperial player spends a lot of time watching and waiting to be a victim to the rest of the players' plots.

I think my primary issue is with this form of tactical game overall - they're very calculating and that has to be from whence the fun derives.

Wanted to love it. Maybe over time (4 missions in) I will.

RATING: 7
Imperial Assault puts you in the midst of the Galactic Civil War between the Rebel Alliance and the Galactic Empire after the destruction of the Death Star over Yavin 4. In this game, you and your friends can participate in two separate games. The campaign game pits the limitless troops and resources of the Galactic Empire against a crack team of elite Rebel operatives as they strive to break the Empireâs hold on the galaxy, while the skirmish game invites you and a friend to muster strike teams and battle head-to-head over conflicting objectives.

As an additional benefit, the Luke Skywalker Ally Pack and the Darth Vader Villain Pack are included within the Imperial Assault Core Set. These figure packs offer sculpted plastic figures alongside additional campaign and skirmish missions that highlight both Luke Skywalker and Darth Vader within Imperial Assault. With these Imperial Assault Figure Packs, you'll find even more missions that allow your heroes to fight alongside these iconic characters from the Star Wars saga.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[u]Comments:[/u]
I have tried both editions of Descent and I have to say this is a much better implementation than either of those behemoths. Out of the box there are definitely a lot of options to play with with both a skirmish mode and a campaign mode to play with. The components, per usual for FFG, are the best in the business. Models are all very detailed and made with some of the best plastic in the business. Colors and distinguishing marks on the map tiles all stand out. The map tiles were done brilliantly and offer a wide variety of possibilities in map generation. Out of the box there are about 40 missions to play, although you may only experience a fraction of those in a campaign. Overall the rules are well done, with one nagging problem of some strange Line of Sight rules that have been pointed out in many different forum posts. My biggest complaint with the game is that it really doesn't feel like Star Wars. All the Star Wars trappings are there, and the models look like they belong in the Star Wars universe, but ultimately what I did in the missions and what actions i took didn't feel anything like the world we have all come to know and love. What you did get was definitely an interesting tactical miniatures game, with a really nice campaign system and numerous choices for the Overlord player. What you didn't get was anything close to an experience of being in one of the movies, and that is a big miss in my book. Overall I definitely enjoyed the game, and would like to play more, but I am glad I didn't purchase this game especially knowing that they will just be doing serial purchases for this game from now on.
