RATING: 7
* About the CAMPAIGN mode:

** Type of game:

$ Miniatures dungeon crawler.

- Most similar game I played: Earth Reborn. Very similar: squad-level skirmishes on usual real life terrain with miniatures in two camps, and missions. However, I prefer the smaller number of figures in Earth Reborn, which makes turns go faster. I also prefer the breadth of flavors in the actions and game play of Earth Reborn. Imperial Assault has the advantage of having an important 1 vs many mode (a la Descent).

$ Somewhat similar game: Space Hulk. But Space Hulk is about a cramped terrain. There is less variety in the maps of Space Hulk, or in the characters, or in the events (no events in Space Hulk). But I like the claustrophobic setting.

$ Somewhat similar game: Claustrophobia. But Claustrophobia is simpler, more streamlined, and the characters move at a much bigger scale.

$ Somewhat similar game: Warhammer: Diskwars. I prefer Diskwars: it is richer in terms of unit powers, tactical choices, and has a nice guessing element (secret goals, command cards).

Pros:

+ Streamlined rules. Easy to pick up. The game can be started by knowing only the main rules, and some details can be explained on the go.

+ Good game flow. The Rebels can confer with each other, which alleviates downtime.

+ 30 missions. I like not knowing what will happen, and the game is replete with unexpected reinforcements and events.

+ The characters have a distinct personality, which is nice for strategizing and making everybody participate to the fight for victory.

+ Very good material. Nice sculpts (not at the level of Space Hulk 20th anniversary edition, with a plastic which is less dense, and shows more mold marks, but very detailed sculpts).

* Cons

- I found the breadth of the decisions a little lacking, for all the glitter: while I liked evaluating the relative strengths of the various threats, developing strategies and make tactical decisions about movement, finding synergies between the characters and using their respective strengths, I almost get the same kind of pleasure from a game like BattleLore on iPad. In a similar category of games, I prefer the thematic richness of Earth Reborn.

- Detail: the stats could use some icons: this would make the first games easier (by allowing the Rebels to quickly spot where is the life of the Imperial troops, etc.).

RATING: 8
The latest iteration of the Descent engine - and it shows, in a good way!
The change in play sequence - alternating activations - does it all. No longer are problems to be analysed from end to end. Instead you need to prioritise, because your  opponent will have an activation before you can take your next one yourself.

Play balance in the campaign still seems to be a bit of a problem: Consensus is that the Imperial player has the upper hand. So for lasting appeal, The immperial might have to pull his punches a bit, which really isn't optimal, as you can end up feeling a bit cheated, even when winning.

RATING: 8
Despite liking fantasy more than space the added skirmish mode almost effectively adds another game to get more bang for your buck, as you can customize your command cards and which units you use. Also, the changes from Descent makes good thematic sense (you can never fail an attack with a blaster but light units are fast enough to dodge, range is now accuracy since a blaster should have near infinite range).

In terms of the campaign the overlord's role has changed to be nearly no card play and instead much more focus on units. The back and forth activation rather than all the heroes go first means the heroes can't just wipe out a third of the overlord's forces before he/she has even gone, and that the overlord can spawn in batches now instead of one at a time is both more realistic and also helps prevent his/her units from being picked off one by one. Instead of having healing characters now anybody can heal themselves via resting. Some things that seemed really powerful in the original (fatiguing really far to get off multiple attacks that could potentially remove your fatigue, blast) have been weakened. 

There also used to be the problem that if someone died the overlord would just camp a monster on its corpse to make it difficult for the hero revive, whereas now you just become wounded and become weaker so that your game isn't practically over, and while killing you once and for all will eliminate you from the current scenario, the overlord is incentivized to attack everyone because if everyone is wounded that is also a win condition.

Finally, only part of the events for each campaign is revealed to the heroes initially, which I think makes your first run through more exciting, while in the future one could instead be more strategic and plan around these traps.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Short note on the history and evolution of this game and its mechanics:

-> Doom: The Board Game + expansions
-> Descent: Journeys in the Dark + expansions
-> Descent: Journeys in the Dark 2. Edition + expansions
-> Star Wars: Imperial Assault

The game mechanics have evolved over several years and many, many expansions. There is no doubt that SW:IA is a very well designed game.

However, I don't enjoy this game as much as I hoped I would. I guess I was looking for a Dungeon Crawler (like Descent) only in a Star Wars setting. As it turns out it is and definitely plays like a tactical miniatures war game and I was not looking for that kind of game.

Even though they did not change much compared to Descent, the few changes do make this game seem a lot more tactical, which sounds good, but ultimately ruins this game for me. You find yourself planing and analyzing a lot, which slows the game down quite a bit. But the worst part in my opinion is that the game seems to only create an "illusion" of being tactical. Ultimately it is still just a "move guy, roll a guy"- type of game. It is far to heavy for actually not offering that much in my opinion.

Also, the campaign never really caught my interest, even though I am a huge Star Wars fan since my childhood.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The theme alone is enough to more than sustain my interest. Over any other source material (LOTR, Hunger Games, Firefly, Star Trek etc) I feel like this is the one that succeeds the most when introducing "nobody" characters as the heroes of the game. Imperial Assault does this with aplomb, with six well-rounded characters that fit classic roles (the leader, the veteran, the smuggler, the sharpshooter, the brawn and the religious/exile). Crafting backstories for them isn't essential, but is very fun in terms of deciding who will do what in the cooperative fashion of the game; "Would my character do this?". The models aren't perfect (some horrible mold lines- Imperial Officers' faces, I'm looking at you..r faces), but for games at a similar price tag, they are well above average. Who doesn't want an AT-ST to assemble and paint?

Not that paint is necessary either- the models look just as good in their natural grey and tan plastic. Personally, I have loved getting stuck into painting the figures and, unlike other miniatures I own, I have started with the rank-and-file rather than the heroes. And I have LOVED it. Star Wars costumes, particularly for Imperials, help to make this a painless and yet as challenging as you want experience.

In terms of gameplay... well, I admit to being a hater of too many rulebooks (hence why this is not a 10). This has just one too many. Granted, this is for different styles of play, but it put me off immediately. Lo-and-behold, there is an app! Much like Mansions of Madness (and there are more than a few similarities, thankfully, to ease me in) the app does all the thinking for you in running the game. Not only that, but the missions appear to be different than the ones found in the campaign book in the box; can anyone say bargain? Perhaps I will find the rulebooks amiable when I can pluck up the courage, but at least for the moment there is an excellent index section for as and when situations arise that need clarification.

The thrill of Darth Vader or IG-88 turning up in the middle of a mission cannot be downplayed and creates utterly gripping moments as two Rebel soldiers bit the force-dust allowing Gaarkhan to flee to safety. It's sequences like these, from different villains, that give this game a real sense of drama and epic scope despite its relatively small skirmish-size structure.

Amazing game. Fully recommended.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Spent time playing skirmish competitively for multiple months, just never had fun playing the game, regardless of how much the concept seemed good in theory. It seemed hard to actually build a list that was both fun and interesting. Then I played the campaign for the first time yesterday and had a miserable experience. The game is completely unbalanced. Random rolls with little in the way of modification is all that tips the game between a completely easy time and an impossible challenge. The campaign is fundamentally unbalanced, and requires the Empire purposefully playing at less than their best for the rebels to stand a chance. In addition, small and semi-random rules are everything in this game, and you will spend more time looking up rules than playing the game. Stay away, play X-Wing instead if you're looking for a Star Wars miniatures game.

RATING: 8.5
Campaign games do nothing for me.  Greatly prefer individual scenarios as it's much easier to get to the table.  With that said, I've still had fun with the campaign in this game.  It's probably a little easier to get a Star Wars campaign going with others than it is a random generic theme.      

Skirmish is the highlight of the game for me.  I've always loved putting together team/squads (which is why Heroscape is my #1 game).  There is a ton of variety in Imperial Assault, once you grab a few expansion packs - various skirmish maps, units, command cards, etc.  Would love more 4 player skirmish maps and objectives that are different than earning 40 VP to win.  

A coop app will make this a hard game to top.

RATING: 8
Imperial Assault is the dungeon crawler I've been looking for.. except it doesn't have dungeons.
IA fixes the biggest issue of Descent; the boring Overlord (Imperial Player in IA) gameplay by alternating the turns between the Imperial player and rebel players. The second biggest change is the story driven structure of the campaign which makes the game enjoyable whether you lose or win the individual scenarios.
Some players fear the possible balance breaking snowball effect if one side wins multiple scenarios in a row as the winner usually gets reward bonuses. While I've yet to face this issue, I can imagine it happening in some rare cases. Nevertheless the game is highly enjoyable and in our plays the winner has always been resolved with the last die roll, so I definitely feel the game is as balanced as possible with all the options it offers.

RATING: 9.5
Best heavy game of 2014.
It's almost too good to be true, Descent 2ed. has been streamlined even further and the generic nerd fantasy theme replaced with a one that pretty much everyone is familiar with - Star Wars. The ultimate dungeon crawler experience. As long as they keep expansions coming this will be the only one you'll need. Descent system of overlord vs. others is great and fixes all "AI" problems other dungeon crawlers have, of course you need a dedicated overlord who isn't just centered on winning alone but wants to create an immersive and fun experience for the group. But yeah it's awesome game. FFG has really stepped up their rulebook game recently with Eldritch Horror and this, and after ~20 minutes of dedicated reading (by overlord) and a quick tutorial mission you'll be all set for campaign. You can also play miniature skirmish matches with same set, good value.

2-Player Comment:
*After some 2p campaign games I would say it's my favored player count in many cases. Other player as emperor or with RedJaks automated co-op variant. The scaling system is very elegant and there's very little downtime with only two. Add skirmish and you have a very solid package for two.

Expansions:
+Star Wars: Imperial Assault - Twin Shadows
+Star Wars: Imperial Assault - Return to Hoth

RATING: 9.5
What can I say?  I was a fan of Descent 2nd Edition and this game is a further refinement of that system, with the added bonus of having a Star Wars theme on top.  The mechanics are more polished than in previous iterations of the system, adding the ability to dodge and cancel surges while improving line-of-sight rules (oddly, the new system was one that I had house ruled for D:2E when it came out), while also incorporating story-like elements seen in games like Mansions of Madness.

Frankly, this game is a real winner, and if you don't detest rolling dice you owe it to yourself to give this one a look.

RATING: 7
Would rate this game higher, but feel that it is an FFG money trap.  The base game does not even include Han Solo, Chewbacca, Yoda, R2D2, C3PO, Leia, etc. etc.  You have to buy these character packs for $10 each.  To me that is like opening a box of Lucky Charms with no Marshmallows, and the instructions saying you can purchase the marshmallows here...

If this game's expansions came out like Descent 1st Edition, than this would be a lot more tempting for me to buy, and also would rate the game higher, maybe an 8 or 8.5.  Plus FFG has been decreasing with each base set the variety.  For instance, in the base games Descent 1st Edition came with 20 Heroes, Descent 2nd Edition had 8 Heroes, and now SW:IA has only 6 Heroes.

The game play is very much a rush to complete these objectives and the heroes win type of game.  With all of the setup required for SW:IA, I would rather play Descent 1st Ed, for a deeper more in-depth dungeon crawl with more loot, monsters and heroes to choose from, or Tannhauser for a faster setup and easier to teach tactile miniatures game.  

With that said, it was fun for a group of old men to go "Pew! Pew!"

I saw this one sale as a âhotnessâ sort of item at Cancon for AUD$170 and while I am a Star Wars fan going back to seeing the original movie at Hoyts in George Street Sydney in 1977 I just could not justify that sort of price tag. Fortunately, my good friends at the Book Depository made sure that I didnât have to as a month or so later they had the base set on sale for AUD$90 odd with free postage. Very hard to say no at that price so I didnât.

Anyway, much as you would expect with a Fantasy Flight Games product, it is very well produced and the plastic miniatures are very very nice and true to the Star Wars characters they represent. I have been thinking about painting some plastic gaming miniatures (as opposed to myâ lead mountainâ of unpainted metal figures for miniatures gaming) and these guys have bumped the Zombicide chaps from the head of the queue.

I took the game along to a local board game meet up (GLOG at Gungahlin Library) in the expectation that a chum who knew the game would be there to teach it to me; alas he wasnât. I was a bit surprised when Little Fish the Elder (aged four) insisted we play this game, despite me only having given the rules a cursory glance (most of my rules reading time had been devoted to Cthulhu Wars). Anyway, Star Wars: Imperial Assault it was so I set up the introductory scenario and was joined by a few other others (one of when was an experienced player in the âdungeon crawlâ whose name escapes me that this is shares similarities with) so it wasnât too much of a learning curve.

Star Wars: Imperial Assault plays pretty well and isnât too hard to pick up. The challenge will be to get the most from oneâs troops as they acquire experience cards and suchlike; that knowledge will come with time and experience. One thing I really do like with Star Wars: Imperial Assault is that it has rules for both one-off skirmish games as well as campaign games. From what I can see, playing out a full campaign will be a very satisfying gaming experience.

So, after one play I am pretty much hooked. I can see me trying to get this to the table again and again as well as purchasing all the expansions and miniatures packs because:

RATING: 3
Imperial Assault is a pretty awful and vastly over hyped re-skin of [i]Descent 2nd Edition[/i]. Sure its chromed to hell with a big grey AT-ST miniature in the box, but in terms of substance its just as awkward and shallow as D2nd. Being Star Wars themed doesn't make it any better!

Just as in D2nd they've killed all the decision making, fun and depth in the process of making a 'faster, scenario driven' game. The timed nature of many scenarios forces both sides hands tactically. You have to play cheesy tactics like blocking and stalling to win, or in the case of Rebels running away so the enemies can't touch you, both of which are frustrating to do and frustrating to have done to you. This rubbish makes every game feel like a soul destroying optimization puzzle, not a entertaining semi-coop game.

Most scenarios are totally unbalanced in favour of one side or the other.

Heroes also overdevelop into obnoxiously over complex piles of skill spaghetti ('Oh I rolled 2 hits and a surge, I'm going to use this skill to add a surge and gain focus, which in turn allows me to use my chazwaser ability to add Pierce 3 and move three spaces, I've then got within three range of a different target so I'm going to shoot them too using a special shot where I can deal damage equal to range but only if I'm stood next to another Hero in which case you can't see me to shoot me back and I deal you 9 damage pierce 3 with bleed, cleave 1 and stun'). Often Imperial player's units are essentially dead in one hit unless they get lucky and roll an evade!

As the Imperial/Overlord you're also significantly nerfed, with no real customization options. You have no event/keeper deck to build, no threat management (spawning points don't count), leaving you with few effective choices. After a point there is also little to spend experience on, as only a handful of cards will have a use depending upon your opponents. Influence cards are so hard to buy and their effects so circumstantial and weak they are hardly worth a glance a lot of the time.

All in all I just hate this game. It makes me feel miserable, bored and unhappy, regardless of which side I play on. Its as fun as throwing yourself off a building so that you land testicles first on a railing.

The 3 is totally justified.

RATING: 8
I own this and several expansions. Having both an RPG and Wargame background, I find this game plays best as the Campaign (RPG lite) game. The skirmish, though interesting, is not an effective system compared to the older SWM - designed for skirmish. I rate this game very high for the Campaign - and it is now the only way we play it. It is also good for children who are 10 or older, or even 8 or older with some "assistance". The coop nature is best done when the Imperial Player views themself as "GM Lite", as opposed to an opponent. This creates the best atmosphere and real RPG style tension.

Overall, excellent game system for RPG Lite concepts. Good way to bring board gamers into the wider RPG fold. Very good for campaign systems.

RATING: 9.1
2-5 players

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.4
This is Descent 2.0 with a Star Wars makeover. Most of the mechanics are exactly the same and some even have the same names (e.g. surges). Having played Descent it would have been less confusing if they hadn't bothered trying to rename the mechanics at all to avoid any confusion when switching between the two games.

Where the dungeon delving theme fits perfectly with the tile exploration and monster fighting in Descent, the Star Wars theme is limited to skirmish style encounters and apart from the highly detailed miniatures does not bring the Star Wars universe to life. So people looking for a deep Star Wars theme maybe disappointed with this dungeon crawl experience.

As I love dungeon crawls I think this is a fantastic game but as I prefer the D&D style setting my rating is lower than my Descent rating.

RATING: 10
Descent was awesome fun and the long play time didn't bother our group. 

That said, FFG have finally nailed the formula with Imperial Assault. The Campaign Play is awesome, the rules refinements are spot on and the experience of each play is tense and well balanced. 

Add to that an entirely additional way to play, the Skirmish Game - read this as 1 v 1 tactical miniatures game, and Imperial Assault is worth every cent of the investment if you are into tactical miniatures types of games. 

Just be prepared to spend a lot of money because if you love it you will want every expansion and wave of minis. 

Descent was the HeroQuest of my middle years and Imperial Assault will be the game that I remember well into my greying years.

RATING: 8
It kind of out-Descents Descent in some tactical aspects, even though the feel is different enough for me to still love Descent, too. I play the campaign exclusively.

The SW theme seems to work great for this one & the combat is a bit more balanced and tactical than in Descent. 

That being said, in some missions (well, with repeated plays feels that maybe in most? my rating is kind of dropping with every play) the Empire can simply flood the game with endless troops, dropping them basically wherever he wants, which goes well with the theme and makes the scenarios "evenish", but at the same time kills the feel of the overall balance and achievement of the game. For me, this kind of difficulty feels artificially maintained, and has a a deus ex machina feel, which I don't care for that much.

RATING: 6
[b]Like:[/b]
-Campaign play where you level up
-Bosses

[b]Dislike:[/b]
-Imbalance that's hard to balance yourself
-Lots of small details in the rules
-Making lots of decisions that are most of the time unimportant (e.g. where to deploy and end your movement)

Okay, so I've played this game A LOT! Every campaign (until and including Hearth of the Empire) have I played at least twice, so you can count how many hours that about is. However, I wouldn't have played this game that much if it wasn't for the people who wanted to play this more than I do. I for one, don't like it that much anymore.

Here is the thing: initially it's pretty cool when you're discovering all the things and systems about this game. You set standards to stuff. So the first time as the Imperial player you deploy an AT-ST or Darth Vader you feel mighty when you suddenly roll 2 defence dice instead of 1, and the Rebels will look in fear and be like "What?! Two defence dice?!". The first time the Rebels destroy one of these they'll feel amazing. Then, the first time they ONE-SHOT (or one activation) one of these they'll feel invincible. The Imperial player will feel almighty again once he deploys his first boss with advanced stats. And once the rebels are able to defeat that, your standards are set in stone, and every game after you'll know what's a threat and what not.

So when nothing is surprising anymore, what kind of game are we left with? The Rebels can try different heroes and new combos, the Imperial player however... will keep doing what he always has been doing? He doesn't really have excess to combos so his role is basically the same as always: choose the most powerful units and cards (the Villain deck in Jabba's Realm might be the only exception). So yes, Imperial player will get boring faster than the Rebels, but that's okay: players can switch or you can use the app.

So what game are we THEN left with? I'll admit, even after my dozens of plays, I still like building my unique heroes and wondering what items will appear in the shop. Playing though, the part that takes the most of your time will become pretty meh. You'll just be doing whatever looks best, with no real interesting decisions. The dice just become an annoying time sink and numbers become meaningless. 90% you know that if you attack that guy twice he will be dead, but you still have to roll to make sure he doesn't get lucky. Dice should be about mitigating risk, but most of your rolls won't be about that anymore, just a resolution.

But wait: dice are also about having random events happen and adapting to them. True, you could see it as an interesting shifting puzzle: what should you do if the guy you intended to kill survives? But I don't want to roll and re-roll and cross-reference, all just to check if the guy survives! As you might've suspected already, skirmish is definitely not a game mode I like.

Okay, I might be rambling a bit, but here is the main thing: The problem is not so much that there are no more decisions to be made, but that the decisions you have to make have such a small difference. Whether you should kill this guy first or this, whether you should end your character's movement/deploy there or the space next to it; it all won't matter 90% of the time, but you still have to decide what it is exactly what you want to do. I find it hard to get my point across but I hope this last bit did.

TLDR; your first 1 or 2 campaigns will be awesome! But if you expect the game to be as new every time you play a new campaign, think again. It will just be more of the same, just with different numbers.

RATING: 6
Darn.  I so wanted this to be awesome.  I played with my fair share of Hasbro Star Wars figurines as a child and this seemed like a great opportunity to pass it on to my kids with more structured rules.  The figs in this game are great!  The boards are great!  I love how you can create scenes from within the Death Star or all the way to Endor.  But the game play is dry.  Your star wars character gets to do two things, which really boils down to moving and shooting or shooting twice.  Tactical nuance attempts to come from different dice based upon the weapon you use and the defense of the target.  But at the end of the day there are just not any real choices to be made.  This game made me realize that I am not a dudes in the hallway gamer, but even with that deficit, this game is not as good as Myth, and is in a galaxy far far away from Mage Knight, which is both an excellent dudes in hallways game and just an amazing game in its own right.

RATING: 9
Wow is this game good. If you're a Star Wars geek, there's no reason why you shouldn't buy this game. The story is immersive and I love all the different twists and turns a session can take. I have only played as the Imperials (and, unfortunately, have not won as often as I'd like), so my experience is entirely from being the GM. If you're the GM, definitely set this game up ahead of time; even then, however, when you move on to additional missions and side missions, the modular map system slows you down a lot. RATING: 5
Hi!

So me and my group just finished the campaign of the base game, with me as the Imperial player. We started out with 4 players and midway through we were 3 until the end.

We did the following missions:
Aftermath - Rebel win
Loose Cannon - Imperial win
A new Threat - Imperial win
Sorry about the mess - Imperial win
Fly Solo - Rebel win
Means of production  - Imperial win
Viper's Den - Rebel win
Homecoming - Imperial win
Captured - Rebel win
Drawn In - Imperial win
A Simple Task - Imperial win
The Source  - Imperial win
Desperate Hour - Rebel win

Rebels - 5 wins
Imperial - 8 wins

Rebels won the campaign

Throughout the campaign the missions felt biased towards the Imperial player having the upper hand.
Most of the wins by the imperial player was a complete walkover and vice versa.
Three of the missions felt like they could have gone either way.

The players used the following characters:
Gidion Argus (left the game midway through the campaign and the rebels gained the heroic card)
Fenn Signis
Diala Passil
Mak Eshka'rey

*SPOILER*

And in the last mission they decided they had lost anyway and added Han Solo as their ally to the mission.
I added Darth Vader to my hand of deployment cards using the agenda card.
They took their time quietly eliminating forces and making sure I never had more than a few groups out.
It became easy to clear each room and by the end General Weiss had no where to hide and it was just a matter of math that he could not sustain.
1Â½ rounds before their time would run out they eliminated him and won the campaign.

In the end I felt that there was no point to this campaign and that the last mission we played, determining the campaign winner, was heavily biased towards the players.
I would have loved a mission which was touch and go and could have gone to either side, but this mission was a slaughter, where the only thing I could do was run and hide, trying to run out the clock.
All the cards I had gained and the power of my troopers amounted to nothing since the players could easily one-shot them, sometimes multiples, because if I was to make a dent on the players standing next to Solo, my troopers would have to be able to reroll.
Giving them a 4-action AT-ST didn't help matters much either. If they didn't have that thing, maybe the situation could have been a bit more even.

*/SPOILER*

Having tried my best to prepare for a win in the campaign, it fell flat for me, now that I've just lost the deciding mission. And going into it with a 8 - 4 score of wins I felt confident that I should have a good chance at least of pulling out a win.
I didn't want another mission of me crushing the players, but I definately didn't want this either.

I don't think that this mission and some of the others have been playtested enough by the designers and when this mission is the one deciding the end result of the campaign, it should be at least a challenge for both sides.

We've had some fun during this campaign, but most of the time the players have felt betrayed by the missions and lastly I felt betrayed by the last mission and the campaign win-condition.
This is why I'm giving this game a considerably lower rating, than I would have otherwise, which could have been helped by a more balanced campaign.

I will probably only play skirmish once or twice with this game, if that. So that part of the game has little to no value for me.
I only hope that we will be given a more open ended system like "Descent: Road to Legend", which was much more open ended and could endure several playthroughs without getting "old".
I don't see myself nor the players playing this campaign twice.


PS: The Gidion Argus-player left because he had to move to a different part of Denmark. Not because of unhappiness with the game :)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Full disclosure: Not a SW fan. I mean, i like it I guess, but I have always seen the franchise as the lowest possible common denominator in terms of nerdery fandom. So IÂ´ve always been wary of "X thing but with Star Wars Theme slapped on OMG!BEstEVR!!!" Reactions.

Having said I love me a Dungeon Crawler/minis game anytime. So when a friend bought this we jumped at the chance to test it out.


Pros:
-Spectacular componentes: Classic FFG Bling here. The Walker mini alone is spectacular. The printing artefacts of FFG's usual cardboard stock however do rob the maps of some of its luster. Games like Level 7 or Claustrophobia look way more vibrant and detailed, but otherwise A+.

-Streamlined Play: Make no mistake, this is Descent 2.5. But there are minor enough changes that make it feel a bit simpler and more streamlined. The dice checks are faster and the turn sequence is altered to make it hero/overlord/hero/overlord instead of all players/Overlord. This does change the strategies in the game but I believe its for the better, and feels like it makes the overlord role more engaging (have only played as hero so far).

-Excellent Campaign play: Make no mistake, no other game in the market comes with a campaign book as comprehensive right out of the box. Branching paths, equipment & skill progression, sidequests, etc. Sure, you could argue that the "storyline" or whatever is barely there, but the SW flavour takes care of the fluff already and the allure is in the comprehensive scenario tree and options presented. A++.

CONS:

-Lackluster missions: Sooner or later it sinks in. The missions are all the same. Not in terms of objectives but on terms of flow. Basically the game exploits the idea that the overlord will always have some scenario-screwing twist locked behind an objective door or timed element. And its a one-trick pony. Once you've seen it it always boils down to "remember that when we open that door, a ton of baddies or some crap like that is going to appear". Bleh.

-Monotonous Gameplay: Overall the gameplay experience is cloned from Descent, which is not 100% to my liking. It can be fun and engaging due to its progression model, but IA's gameplay can basically be summed up as a "smash Â´n grab" race. Basically you have a very tight window of turns in which to complete your objectives and the overlord just keeps pumping monsters your way. Cool. Except the maps are small affairs (due to the time limits) that only serve to funnel characters into fights constantly. There's no defensive or conservative play here as in games like Level 7 or Space Hulk. Any turn you are not moving forward and kicking ass is a turn wasted. Which sounds awesome but gets monotonous reaaal fast. Remember how dynamics work kids: Something can only be perceived as "fast" if you alternate it next to something slower.

-Questionable Mechanics: You might consider these pet peeves, but if you are into miniature/skirmish games you would do well to remember that a)LoS rules are basically broken and b)movement is ridiculous.
Basically LoS is calculated from an attacker's corner to two target corners. But you can count valid corners as those within a wall and or in any configuration. Similar to D&D but without the concepts of partial cover, which results in basically everyone always having LoS against anyone.
Seriously.
I dare you to check this, but unless you are basically puting a whole building between your enemies there's practically zero chance you can take cover/hide from enemy fire.
Which leads me to the fact that there's no real way to block or hinder movement as any character can move across any diagonal even if flanked on all corners by walls and enemy units. Oh, and there's no facing except for some giant enemy units. 

Again this all falls in line with the idea of the game being a race and nothing getting in the way of you finding and shooting your opponents. But its cheesy as hell and robs the game of any tactical edge whatsoever.

-Maps are simple and small: Not to be redundant, but the largest maps we played so far are still smaller than Space Hulk's intro mission. Yes, at first glance SH's puny Suicide Mission is still larger than anything in this game :S. Again, this is because the game is a race and has no exploration or defensive play whatsoever, but stil...

Overall: If you are insanely in love w/Descent or SW then of course this just begs to be bought, and that's what FFG is counting on. However people need to understand that the game is merely engaging because of the campaign book and its progression system. And that's a one-trick pony. Besides that you have a mostly blunt game with zero room for subtlety or tactical nuances, which can still be fun, but puts it miles behind games that offer the same kind of gameplay with more elegant mechanics and variety of play and tactical options. No matter how you slice it Imperial Assault is a broken record that keeps blaring the same notes over and over. They may be fun notes for some, but better experiences are found elsewhere regardles of your undying loyalty to Disney's franchise.

RATING: 5

RATING: 7
This is a simple skirmish game that uses most of the Descent 2 rules. It has a 1vs1 confrontation mode game that seems pretty interesting, but we just played the first "mission". Of course, the "star" of this game must be the campaign. I like the player scale mechanisms.

I cannot say the game progresses slow, but it doesn't feel fast either, because you're not doing anything when it's not your turn.

The components are top notch. The miniatures are really cool and the rest of the stuff has high quality. Sadly, the price is even higher and just by having a look at the planned expansions, their cost and their meager components, it feels FFG is more interested in making money than in making a good game.

After being a RPG hardcore gamer during a lot of years, this kind of games doesn't fill me up. Yes, the decisions are there, they are meaningful, even if not deep, there is tactic, but RPG have soooo much more than that... Still, they have some entertaining value for me and I can play them.

Of course, I know that this game will be an instant hit for many ameristrash lovers. I wish I could enjoy it as much as they, because it's Star Wars we're talking about :D

RATING: 10
This is one of my favorite games ever played, even if it did not have the Star Wars theme. I feel that it's a much more polished version of Descent 2nd Edition and it fixed the things that I thought made no sense in Descent. Also, it's two different games in one box; two different and great games I might add.

To help those of you who are thinking about this game I included my comparisons between both, Star Wars: Imperial Assault (SW:IA) and Descent 2nd Edition:

* 2 Different and Great games in 1 box (Traditional Campaign & 2-Player Skirmish mode); Descent is only one game (Campaign)

* SW:IA has a better hero kill mechanic where the first time you deal full damage to a hero he is "wounded" (flip his card over and has weaker stats), if you deal full damage to a wounded hero then he is out for the remainder of the mission. I was not pleased with the Descent as a hero NEVER dies!!! When you âknock-outâ a hero in Descent, the hero simply loses a turn and comes back to full health, something I found very frustrating as the Overlord player

* In SW:IA the additional ally/villain packs add more to the game in the form of Skirmish & Story/Side Missions; not just an additional figure which you may not be able to use in a campaign like in Descent 

* SW:IA has extremely high replay value as there a countless number of story/agenda cards, and the number grows as you add expansions

* The mechanic of taking turns in SW:IA is much better and polished; activating one Rebel, then one Imperial, one Rebel and so forth, makes it feel like each player is involved in making actions every other turn. Unlike Descent where all Heroes perform their actions, then all monsters, all Heroes, all monsters, so forth.

* For players who are inexperienced to dungeon crawlers, SW:IA might be a little too intimadating and complicated for them at first. Descent is a tad bit simpler if you want to get newbies interested

* Both games contain a ton pieces to organize and setup, SW:IA has more

* Descent instructions are a little bit easier to read and understand the first time

RATING: 9.5
Right now this game is completely blowing me away. It looks amazing, (the components {especially the map pieces} are top-notch!) it plays super-streamlined and fun, and the campaign is so fricking cool I want to pee. But I won't. 

Right now I am having an incredible time with this game. I cannot speak to balance at this time, but the Imperial player did kind of run away with the first campaign I played. I think that is because of the one flaw I can find with the gameplay: Rebel Allies cost too much Threat to deploy. They just aren't worth it. I mean, Rebels got Luke Skywalker on the first side mission, and then deployed him in the next few missions and lost every single one. First mission they did not deploy him, they won (unfortunately for them it was a forced mission and it didn't get them anything). In theory, he has to kill 10 Threat worth of Imperial units to make him worthwhile. It's just not going to happen. ]

Other than the Rebel Allies foible, I feel that this game is the perfect adaptation of the good team vs. bad guy game. I think Middle-Earth Quest is a better one-off game, but the campaign mode is just so well put together and slick that overall this game is better. Time will tell if this remains true, but as for right now, I am foaming at the mouth to buy all of the add-ons for this, as soon as they come out. Looks like you've got another money maker, FF.

Thank you, based Conieczka.

RATING: 7
What was initially a fun and exciting Star Wars themed miniature game quickly grew stale for my group.  After playing through 3 campaigns, this one is going back into the box until the co-op app comes out.  

The reason for this is in the balance of the missions.  As a player, there are few clues in the mission to tell the players whether this is a "race to the finish" or a "hunker down and methodically kill your way to the finish" mission.  If you guess incorrectly, you will lose.  In "race to the finish" missions, you need to sprint to the objective as quickly as possible, often ignoring enemies.  In "hunker down and methodically kill your way to the finish" scenarios, you need to take it slowly and kill every enemy you see in order to survive.  You can see how guessing incorrectly will lose you the game.

Also, the players equipment is entirely luck of the draw, and it's easy for the Imperial player to grow more powerful than the group.  In none of our games has the Imperial player lost.  Since they know exactly what the mission entails and what the objectives are, they can easily tailor their choices accordingly.  This is not to suggest that it's impossible for the Rebel player to win, but it's extremely difficult.  I hope the app changes that.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 4
It's a medium-weight dungeon crawl.  

Is it a bad game? No. 
Does it have anything to do with Star Wars? No.  

This is one of the most pernicious examples of a pasted-on theme that I've ever seen. Probe droids wandering around in a cargo ship?  Imperial Guard hanging around a security installation?  Everything is just so shoe-horned in to make use of the Descent 2 mechanics.  

You want to do a Star Wars game?  Give me wide-open spaces and deep hallways, hangars and chasms and long-distance fire-fights. This is just appropriating a theme to sell an interesting, but unrelated product.

RATING: 9
IA is a mechanically sound, highly enjoyable tactical game. It is based on Descent, which I believe it improves upon in all aspects. Although strategy is certainly involved, the random dice rolls and variety of effective options prevents you from thinking too hard about the next "perfect move." Instead, you jump in, try your best, and hope your buddies bail you out if you messed up. :-) Great fun! Highly recommend playing the Star Wars soundtrack in the background while playing.

RATING: 10
I've never played Descent so I can't comment on the similarities or contrasts there.  I really like the combat mechanics in this game.  I find them very fast and easy to process.  I also like the good guy/bad guy/good guy/... turn sequence.  I wish the campaign for the base box wasn't so long. RATING: 8
The Star Wars theme is fine, but I honestly like the Descent Fantasy theme more. Regardless, the minis are nice, the levels are well designed, the changes to the rules drastically improve the game system over it's mechanical predecessor, Descent 2nd edition. The huge improvements include having players take turns between monster activations, moving the miss result onto the defense die that the lighter armored characters roll, as opposed to having it on the attack die, and in general, the theme fits the timed-style maps much better than a Dungeon Crawl setting does.  That being said, my playgroup took issue with the timed missions aspect of things. For them, this game needed to be more of a monster-masher for them to enjoy it. Pity.

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:star: Multiple plays stay interesting
:star: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::star::nostar::nostar: Raw enjoyment

RATING: 5
Of the myriad of dungeon delving scenario games out there, (Doom, Descent, Conan, etc.) I think Imp Assault is one of the better.
The rules are polished so it's easy to just get going from scenario to scenario, and while I'm no Star Wars fan the theme is used here to great effect...

That said, Imperial Assault still suffers from the same issues that turn me off other games in this style.  It takes hours to finish a scenario, and you'll reflect back to realize not much at all actually happened.   The campaign story snippets would be better placed in children's cartoons, and every decision is followed by lots of rolling dice negating any semblance of meaningful player input.

RATING: 10
I bought this on a whim when it was on sale after hearing it was pretty good. It quickly became one of my favorite games, if not my absolute favorite game. The asymmetrical, mostly-cooperative gameplay is cool, and it feels like a very light role-playing game, focused mostly on combat with some light storytelling. It being set in the Star Wars universe only makes it that much cooler, in my opinion. Setup takes a decent amount of time and it was a little hard for some of my friends to get it at first, but once you get into it, the rules aren't that complicated.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
While this game has some interesting ideas, it suffers from a couple of flaws, some serious.

The early-game of a campaign is heavily Imperial-sided, with the Rebel players having limited access to weapons and items. This, fortunately, becomes less of a problem in the later stages.

The most serious flaw is that the game was designed for multiple playthroughs, but not in the traditional sense: while it has limited scripted variations, the main concept is designed so that Rebel players are at a serious disadvantage the first time, as they don't know how turn limits and several other triggers affect their games, which in turn gives the Imperial player a great advantage, as they can plan ahead.

The aspect of cooperation between the Rebel players is, however, well executed: this makes it a still enjoyable experience when played with friends.

RATING: 6
I can't decide if I like this more than Descent 2 or not. The new turn order, where there's more back-and-forth between good and evil, means less endless chat from the players as they try to decide their entire teams movement ... but in a way it also reduces the skill level in Imperial Assault, with planning reduced.

It succeeds in making small team combat tactical.

Overall, it's just move-attack, move-attack over and over again, with turns taking surprisingly long to resolve because of table chat. 

A pretty good game but none of it is rocket science. Unless you get into the 'spirit' of it, you might just feel there's not much game inside the huge box.

RATING: 4
I feel kinda weird not giving this a higher score.

It has lots of things I like. Campaign based progression. Fantastic miniatures (that really just scream at me to paint). Team based play. Strategy required from the players. It's hugely thematic (it's Star Wars!!!!). Gameplay makes sense and is pretty easy to learn. It's a nice looking dungeon crawler. Seriously, all good here!

On the other hand, I currently don't understand how the rebels can win. Admittedly this is only from 2 short campaign missions. 

My issue is really with my final game - the Imperial player won one game by completely surrounding our last mission objective. Since we couldn't interact with it if there is an Imperial soldier within 4 spaces - we had to be able to kill every single one of them. The ability to do this kind of thing really bursts the thematic bubble. I can just imagine the conversations of the actual characters in game.

Stormtrooper - "Captain, the rebels are coming. Shall we move forward and take them down?"
Royal Guard - "No, they're coming for this terminal. Lets huddle around it and prepare Operation Human Shield"
ST - "Errrr, OK. Can I at least shoot at that guy?"
RG - "NOOOO! He's a Wookie. If you hit him, you'll only make him mad and he'll be able to hit harder."
ST - "What about that trooper over there? I'm sure I could hit him."
RG - "NOOOO! He's at full strain. If you hit him, you won't do much damage and he'll likely lose all his strain tokens. Just put your gun down and go huddle beside those Tusken Raiders"
ST - "......"

Perhaps it is partly to do with our tactics (though some of the guys had played this game a lot previously). I do appreciate that it is a successful tactic for the Imperial player to use and it isn't a case of being a sore loser. It just takes some of the mystique of the game away to see this kind of tactic be usable and successful.

I will be playing this again and I will certainly reconsider re-scoring this in the future. 

*Edit after 2nd night's play
Well, now been on the end of a Rebel victory. "Focus on the objectives" is certainly the call of the day. More than ever, I realise the game is not about shooting or killing anyone as a Rebel. It tends to always be about just sprinting like crazy to the objective markers. For the Imperial player, it is definitely about swarming and surrounding the objective token with as many bodies as he can fit there and going for victory by attrition. If the Imperial player could have opened the final door, he would have done so and just surrounded the final objective with as many people as he could and hoped to stall us until time ran out. 

So when there isn't really any other tactic for the Rebels except to sprint forward and ignore the people shooting at them, it really makes for very uninteresting decision making. In an entire game, which might last 2 hours+, you might have 2 or 3 actual decisions to make. The biggest decision will be what to spend your end money/XP on.

Also, when it isn't your turn - there's little reason to pay attention to what is going on. So long as the Imperial player wakes you up whenever he shoots your character - that basically sums up what you need to do. Unless you want to be the table captain, of course, and tell everyone else why their idea is wrong and what they should be doing. 

My review now changes to a 4. I'm going to finish the mini-campaign I'm in, then will require serious persuasion to ever bother playing again. Though could possibly change if I ever try Skirmish mode, that sounds like it might possibly be interesting.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6.5
I've played the tutorial and the "Aftermath" mission in the campaign.  Within 1 minute of opening the box 2 of the probe minis were destroyed by my kids (warning for parents).

Pros 
- The persistence of the campaign mode seems to be the main draw for the game.  I will update my review when I experience a few more missions.
- Stellar components (other than the flimsy probes)
- Fantastic presentation
- Intuitive mechanisms for young kids to pick up (though this is countered by the amount of text in the game)
- Miniatures provide a more visceral experience.

Cons
- Setup is long. You have to build the map.  This involves sifting through a pile of tiles finding the correct ones and then assembling it.  As the imperial player you have to then read the campaign text.  You have to find the cards you need for your deployments, find and prepare various other decks and tiles.
- Cross-referencing 3 rulebooks - I'm sure this goes away with experience, but when you are new, you have a rules reference guide, a learn to play guide, and a campaign guide, all of which are interconnected and reference each other.  Example: "What does stun do?"... lets check the learn to play guide, it just says there are effects that are placed on characters.  Ok, let's check the  rules reference, it says check the effect cards... which deck is the effect deck?  Ok, let's look up what the effect deck looks like on the component list in the learn to play guide.  Sort through the massive pile of cards and find the effect deck, find the stun card, read it and place it on a character.  It's a lot of work to figure out what the stun effect does.

Anyhow, I'm excited to keep playing and seeing if the setup and referencing are less time consuming in future plays.

RATING: 10
06-01-2015. Rated after 8 plays. What is there more to say than this game basically is Descent 2 fixed, mating with Star Wars.

Yeah, it's mostly Descent 2. But it's line of sight system is better, the chance to miss moved to the defense die, there are now multiple defense dice (If I remember correctly I think there was just one before). For me, the biggest improvement is the open-endedness of the campaign system, which feels less scripted than Descent as the overlord has events, can spawn/repair units.

This game took everything I disliked about D2, and fixed it. It's a great story-driven game. One potential downside would be that overlord has become either less powerful, or more reliant on the story. Therefore, it seems less replayable.

RATING: 8
:thumbsup: A two-for-one of enjoyable games. The Campaign is a fun Star Wars game, and Skirmish is a true sandbox in which fans can play out the free for all fan fiction fights from their dreams.
:thumbsup: Nice miniatures, a pleasure for painters.
:thumbsup: A double rulebook system: Learn to Play + Rules Reference Guide. This wouldn't work well with many other games, but it does in this FFG title. It reminds me a bit of the videogame approach, where more gameplay abilities are unlocked as the game's storyline progresses. Here you start playing after reading through the Learn to Play booklet, and when you encounter a situation that isn't covered there you look it up in the Rules Reference Guide. The latter comes with a useful index, and a good cross reference system between related items. But as as a rulebook on itself, it's a mess. It's a bit of a nuisance to read it as is. And if you don't encounter the situations that prompt you to look up a rule in there, it's possible that you'll be playing without an essential (albeit often small) rule unwittingly...

----

:thumbsdown: So. Many. Expansions. It's a double-edged knife. Granted, you're not obliged to buy any expansions. You can use tokens and the stat cards are provided in the game (and campaign expansion boxes). But you'll want them, 'cause you're a Star Wars fan. But then you'll have the luxury problem of finding storage for all those miniatures (especially if you paint them), cards and tokens. And the choices: what card decks will a use in my campaign?
 Which figures? For Skirmish it's a lot of fun to be able to build a wide variety of teams, of course.
:thumbsdown: I find Campaign games to be a tad 'bittersweet'. Playing as a campaign adds an extra dimension to the gameplay of any game. Your sessions feel like part of a greater whole, your actions are steps towards something beyond the current scenario. But it adds more 'scheduling' to your free time. The schedules of a campaign group need to align, and that's not something that's easily doable for some people. I have started multiple campaigns, but haven't seen even one of them all the way through to the end... And story-wise, the patchwork of story missions and side missions often provides a narrative that doens't make all that much sense. Though I must add that each mission on its own reall does feel like Star Wars.

RATING: 6
Imperial Assault is the best implementation yet of the "Descent" system, sanding away some of the rough edges that have always given me pause before. The gameplay is leaner and meaner than it was even in Descent 2e, and the Star Wars setting is well done enough, though not as natural a fit as something like X-Wing. The scenario design has so far been better in the story missions than in the side missions, though even in the low points I've enjoyed myself. I also question some of the balance with two heroes, who are given extra action and increased HP to help balance the game. It seems a bit strong, though it's also a distinct possibility that I'm just a lousy Imperial player.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
This is a game that only true fans of Descent and other dungeon crawlers can appreciate. Anyone else, expecially real RPG players, will wonder why a game based almost 100% on combat got so scanty, confusing and flat rules. Cover and line of fire rules are amusingly messy and explained in great length and detail, but at the end of the day you can either shoot someone or not all, with no middle ground (shoot with penalties) as anyone would expect in the case of partial cover or limited vision. Similarly, movement rules covers almost any possible case (mostly regarding movement through angles and other miniatures), but at the end of the day, any miniature can always move through almost everything, albeit with a penalty. Concepts like interdiction, suppressing fire or area control are completely absent (strangely enough, however, heroes can heal themselves while shooting by getting special results on the dices), and almost any tactical decision is more based on the intrinsic mechanics of the game (mostly how many rounds are left before the failure of the mission) than on something that really resemble tactical movement in the middle of a shootout. 
In other words, this is a game that is incredibly simplified, made for players who likes to play a campaing and getting special powers for their characters while more or less stomping any opposition. Anyone else who may be interested in a mode realistic combat simulation, should look elsewhere.

RATING: 7.5
Improvements over Descent 2nd Edition:
+ The X die result moved to one of the defense dice, to which not every figure has access to (only those without armor)
+ Instead of the questionable reinforcement rules (discouraging from combat), the "Overlord" now again uses threat tokens
+ After one of the hero players performed his turn, the Imperial player activates one of his figure groups (more interaction)
+ Every hero can rest to heal strain (surges) and/or damage on himself
+ Spending strain for movement is limited to 2 movement points per activation
+ Heroes have a wounded state with decreased stats. If all heroes are wounded, the Overlord often wins.
+ Attributes have an associated die instead of fixed numbers per hero
+ Line of sight is now traced from one attacker space corner to two defender space corners (non-reciprocal)
+ Player activations scale based on the number of players (same number of activations for all player counts)
+ The campaign guide is hidden information like in Mansions of Madness
+ There are side missions for both sides, explaining the back story of the characters, with unique rewards
+ The overlord chooses cards from 1 of 3 class decks providing abilities that are always available once purchased
+ Figures can move through enemy figures (at higher movement cost), so large figures cannot block passageways
+ The scenarios are no longer divided into two encounters, where the 2nd one often was impossible to win after losing the 1st
+ Skirmish mode is an excellent addition and a great alternative to the campaign game for two players

However, sadly, the game is again unbalanced. Some missions are nearly impossible to win for a certain side. FFG needs to improve their playtesting a lot, since this is a general problem present in ALL of their scenario-based games: Descent 1st Edition, Descent 2nd Edition, Battles of Westeros, Mansions of Madness, Tide of Iron, TannhÃ¤user, Doom (2004), DOOM (2016) ... all suffer from balance problems.

RATING: 6
This is possibly a properly developed game, building on much Descent experience, and it does look awesome. Matter is, I was never that impressed with Descent, and this borrowed most of its fatal flaws (to my taste). Adventures and campaigns may well be balanced and play smoothly, as games, but they have little to no simulation value, which is a big deal in something that should draw you into a galaxy far far away: incremental damage in the face of blasters and lightsabers is ridiculous (are these dudes being slowly grilled?), wandering around to find junk in crates when you're out on a sharp, well defined military mission is a misplaced dungeon crawling notion. Jumping over enemy figures, while it may work well as gaming solution, is not really representative of this kind of skirmishing with heavy infantry weaponry (aside from some Jedi acrobatics). At least they got rid of characters coming back after having gone to town, or whatever that was supposed to represent.

Even as a game, although I don't doubt it implies some relevant tactical and even strategic decisions, I don't find all that interesting.

And then, the flavour and storyline...as with most FFG products, these are plain laughable (Does anyone care about General Weiss, really!!?? Give me a break!!!! and don't even get me started on the flavour text...). In the end, many fast paced encounters do not equate to one big epic experience, in my book. Hence, I'm still waiting for the real SW 2-player grand strategy game, a la WOTR. But that's not the direction the hobby is taking, so my wait is likely to be indefinitely long. This is turning more into a general rant against the zeitgeist than anything else.

But then, all in all, I do want to own a big box with plastic Imperial Walker, wookies, and Darth Vader inside.

RATING: 9
I'm a big fan of 1vAll dungeon crawlers, and this is a good one. 

However, as a campaign game, it needs consistent players for campaign to deliver on its promises, which makes it not adaptable to any group or setting. For that reason I have a preference towards skirmish dungeon crawls like Claustrophobia or Super Dungeon Explore) at the moment.

But the immersion of a campaign game can't really be matched by one-shot scenarios if you have the group for it.

Feel of the game: Most missions I've played so far have you carefully choosing between pushing for your objective and taking damage, or stopping to kill stuff but slowing your progress. The heroes are very capable of killing everything, but don't have the time to do so. So it has sort of a "racey" feel with some exploration elements. Definitely not a pure combat game if that's what you're looking for; at times, some of my teammates seem slightly disappointed when I urge them towards the finish line while they'd rather kill something or avoid damage.

[hr]The balance between objectives and combat actually feels pretty damn good I think.I think this game is firing on all cylinders as far as achieving its goals goes, so upping design rating to 5.

Enjoying the campaign immensely.

(5 Design + 4 Enjoyment)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is Star Wars Descent.  You are going to hear a lot of people saying that it is a totally different game and a new experience.  And to be fair, I think this is better than Descent and does a few things to fix some of the problems I had with that game which I otherwise really enjoyed.  But it is still just Descent with a Star Wars theme.

That's not a bad thing mind you.  I love Star Wars and I've got a ton of fantasy themed games so the Star Wars universe is an interesting change to the forumla that I quite enjoyed.  The game is played, if you've never played Descent before, with one player controlling the imperial Forces and 1-4 players controlling the rebel forces in a tactical battle/adventure campaign.  The campaigns are a series of missions that can be anything from straight up "eliminate the enemy forces" goals to escort or fetch missions.  There is also a persistence element which really gives the game replay value.  After each mission in a campaign both the imperial player and the rebel players will acquire new equipment and powers to improve them in future missions and the campaign is meant to form a cohesive story...which it doesn't really do.  It feels more like playing a video game RPG.  There are unrelated side quests and the main story itself jumps around but the persistence angle is still kind of nice.

The one main change from Descent that I enjoy most is the way alternating turns work.  In Descent the heroes would all get to act before the Overlord could act and a lot of times that ended with the Overlord behind on the board right from the get-go.  In this game the heroes and imperial player take alternating turns with each of their units instead of going all at once.  This allows for more tactical flow and balanced turns which of course makes the game more fun for everyone involved on both sides.

There is also a skirmish mode which is nice if you aren't interested in the campaign or just want another way to play with this product as a "one-of".  The skirmish mode is just a turn-based tactical battle between two players.  There are still scenarios that dictate the goals of that particular play and you can build armies yourself with certain amounts of points and special ability cards.  It's a nice addition, especially if you like games like that and want one with Star Wars characters.

I really do enjoy this game but probably won't follow it like I tried to follow Descent.  It is just too difficult to get a group together for the persistent campaign which is the main selling point and if you fool yourself into thinking you can it can be expensive to keep up with a Fantasy Flight product like this with all the character packs and expansions they release continually.

RATING: 9
Being a lifelong fan of Star Wars in general, it didn't take alot of time for me to buy myself a boardgame connected to SW.

Having 4 players trying to beat 1 evil player makes it a wonderfull experience! The game has different stages and missions to complete making it a very long game.
The setup itself takes around 15 mins/mission, and if you're playing with people that overthink every single step, one mission can take up to 1,5 - 2 hours.

The game itself also gets regular (mini)expansions to upgrade your basegame. Having played the campaign multiple times (replayability is high), it's highly suggested buying some of the expansions afterwards. This gives you even more replayability making it a game you can play for weeks. However the pricetag on some of these mini-expansions (mostly characters + 1 extra mission) are somewhat high for not really needing them. The big expansions make the game even longer, and by buying these you'll be able to play the next set of mini-expanions.

However, it's not needed to buy the expansions if you have multiple groups of people! But having played the campaign 3 times (having different results and missions sometimes) makes me want to buy the expansions nonetheless.

So if you're into SW, you'll find this one of the better boardgames out there in the SW universe!

RATING: 9
Never played Descent so all the comments comparing these games are pretty useless.
Played 3 campaign games plus tutorial with 2 other players controlling 2 rebel heroes each. The players take the roll of rebel operatives embarking on missions for the Alliance. Initial campaign involves investigating the development of a new weapon by the Galactic Empire.
Gameplay occurs on a modular map/board formed by interlocking tiles that are set up according to a diagram in the campaign book. One player plays the bad guys (Imperial forces). There is a lot of running and gunning and combat is a main part of the game. Results are determined by rolling certain coloured dice that have diferent symbols on their faces.
Pros: - Star Wars
- Campaign games make for a nice overall focus of your time. Every time you play the game you're moving the story forward.
- Developing characters through skill upgrades and purchasing items
- well designed tiles are strong and fit together solidly and snugly
- nice minis
- lots of options for expansions 
Cons: - thematically, I'm not happy with some choices Fantasy Flight made for enemies. Probe droids guarding Imperial facilities? Royal guards hanging out on random planets?
- Setup can be long if you're not organized
- I sort my games pieces into baggies and sleeve my cards regardless, but you pretty much have to with this game seeing as there is no viable storage option in the box
- I like the idea of campaigns but I'm not sure I really care about this particular story. Also, when we finish what do we do next? There is replayability but the main story stays the same. What do you do when you finish a video game?
- lots of options for expansions that will cost $$

RATING: 8
A very enjoyable tactical skirmish game. I have played 2 campaigns so far and am midway through a third. Overall, I find the game an excellent step up from Descent: Journeys in the Dark (Second Edition). It changed some of the minor annoying rules in that game for the better and one major rule that helps the experience. It's no longer a "Heroes go, then monsters go" activation system, which I think is great for preventing players from becoming bored, and I especially like the change to put dodges on defense dice instead of misses on attack dice. There was usually nothing more deflating than missing an attack. 

That said, there are still some cracks in the faceplate. It is still not well-balanced; some missions will be entirely hopeless for the Rebels if the Imperial player is anywhere near competent. While the hidden mission design is interesting for the Imperial player, I often find it frustrating for the Rebels. For example, there was one mission my group played from one of the figure packs where the Rebels had to escort C-3PO down a corridor with rooms off to the side. They opened one of the side rooms and triggered some enemy spawns. There was a little tunnel linking the two rooms and the other room was locked, so they thought it would be a good idea to put C-3PO in the other locked room as they figured nothing would spawn in there until they decided to open the exterior door. Little beknownst to them, there actually was an active spawn point in that room that I didn't have to tell them about because of the mission secrecy rules, so I ended up spawning a Tusken Raider and defeating C-3PO in one fell swoop. This actually led to the abortion of that campaign, as two of the Rebel players were so incensed by the move that they didn't want to play anymore. Ultimately, I have to admit it seems a little unfair to have a game with missions designed purely around the Rebels activating unknown events that can completely wreck them. It trivializes future campaigns; Rebels will simply plan better once they know in advance, which makes the decision to keep quest information hidden suspect. 

I do like the game a lot, but I admit I am also turned off by the business model. I may pick up the Return to Hoth expansion, but I am turned off from buying anymore figure packs. It just doesn't feel like you get a requisite amount of value to the cost outlay and I don't play the 2 player Skirmish mode, so half of every figure pack is a giant waste of air for me. I really wish the Skirmish mode had been spun off into its own separate game.

RATING: 10
The campaign version of this game is very detailed with a lot of theme and a fantastic story arc. This game is as close to an RPG as you can possibly get while still calling it a board game. The Imperial player essentially serves as the GM and oversees one of several dozen missions provided with the game. Both the Imperial and Rebel players receive XP and credits to spend on various equipment, abilities, and encounters as they advance through the campaign. The game also comes with skirmish or combat rules should players simply wish to battle it out. The combat rules are also used in the campaign game and are easy to understand, yet very well done.

RATING: N/A
Absolutely no-brainer. I want this. 

However... it is just too damn expensive. Over â¬75,- for an incomplete game. Ridiculous.

Yes, it got the 'hot' factor. Yes, it got Star Wars. Yes, it got a lot of miniatures, but so does a secondhand copy of DnD The Adventury Game which I can pick up for â¬5,-. Yes, it builds on a great system (Descent 2.0) and improves on it on some points (most notably the turn structure). Yet...

The box is oversized to justify the ridiculous selling price. Oh wait, FFG tells me "there should be air" because you will buy expansions. Right? Oh right, you've spent already a â¬75,- on this game. Ah well, we know you'll be stupid enough to not remember that if a seemingly decent but factually overpriced expansion comes knocking. We have already six lined up and as we are FFG, you can count on us to expand this game untill you, your friends and even we are sick of it.

By then, you have shelled out massive amounts of money on this game, it has become a completely unplayable piece of junk (Arkham Horror, anyone?) and we move on to the next 'biggest thing'.
Perhaps bringing out a 'deluxe version' of the base + some random (complete) set of expansions so far in order to really piss off early adapters.

Feel some FFG frustration here? You bet. I love a game being complete, playable from the base and no expansions needed anymore. That is why I will ALWAYS favor Battlelore 1st ed over second. Or Thunderstone over advanced. Or any other discontinued line in my book. I hate a company that treats its customer as the stupid money sheep they time and time again prove to be. Even though it puts out great games with great components I love to play.

Now why did I start this rant? Because this game is not only set on the 'premium' end of the price-point, but they deliberately left out pieces out of the base box! Counters, instead of minis, which are only to be had if you shell out more money. It feels like a slap in the face, hitting harder because you've already spent so much money and are 'locked in' into liking this game.

Its like buying a Ferrari. After you've spent your life-savings on it, it will take some time before you are emotionally able to admit it is a crap car on the road. Now, I'm not saying Imperial Assault is a crap game (it wouldn't be high on my wish list if it was), but I find it insulting that a games company puts out an incomplete game at such a price point. 

Will be hunting for a second hand copy, hopefully including all the expansions to make the base game playable (without introducing more holes to patch with other expansions) at a reasonable price point. Will this take a long time? Probably. But I can wait as there are plenty of other great games to go around.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
I like the idea of Imperial Assault, perhaps more than I actually enjoyed the game. We played 5 player scenarios; that means that each player (excluding the DM) takes 1 turn in 10. That's too much downtime for a light-ish dungeon crawl.

There's also far too many tokens and statuses, in classic FFG style. The scenarios also seem fairly random - with the hidden spawn points having a real impact on the game depending on which route you take.

I've recently been playing Heroscape and it's amazing how clean the design of that is compared to this.

Still, I fairly enjoyed this and I'd happily play it 2-3 player in future. I won't be buying it though - when I went to log it, it would appear that there are already 5 or 6 expansions planned.

Update: I don't like it with 5 players. Especially if you're playing as the (not quite a) Jedi character. Too many of her turns are move-attack or even attack-attack again. Rolling 2 dice twice every ten turns is not enough decisions in a 90 minute game. With 2 (playing campaigns) it is swamped with cards and text. 3-4 players might be the sweet spot. At that player count, it's a pretty good game. 

Though I'm still pretty convinced that the scenarios are massively imbalanced in places and deciding whether or not to even attempt a secondary objective can condemn the entire mission to failure, or sometimes there's no hope to begin with.

ImpAss is still very much a case of "fun, but..."

Update: Just play the skirmish game instead. I'm pretty sure that's what this is mostly intended as.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Here are five reasons I am not a fan:

1) First, the games story seems shallow.  I will admit, we only started playing this game recently, but I would have expected a more detailed intro about who the characters were, what it was we were doing, perhaps more detail surrounding what caused us to be going on all of these missions, or even what caused them to meet one another.  Worst of all, the story seems like something I could write in 10 minutes right before I left to play games with my friends, it lacks complexity and feels like a generic MMO mission.

2) It tries to be a tactical game while allowing for completely blind changes.  The whole idea of having unknown information, especially information which allows large amounts of imperials to be summoned, makes it almost impossible to plan ahead, which is the very definition of tactics.  It essentially turns into a luck game of what corridor you decided to run down, or if you did or didn't keep your people together since you have no idea what will happen.

That being said, random chance is okay when the chance has bounds placed on it.  As an example, when I play a tactical game like Warhammer 40K, I know the die can't go above 6, and I have an idea how many they roll.  Even when playing a card game, I have an idea of the cards that might be drawn.  True unknown information like plot twists and major power-shifts belong in a true story RPG with a DM not a game more about tactics and positioning than story.

3) Because players can't check everything the imperial is playing (or at least so I am told) there is no good ability to fact-check rules against the imperial player, which makes point 4 worse:

4) The movement and line-of-sight mechanics are sometimes to the point of not making any sense at all.  Warhammer for example, has complex movement and line-of-sight, but it is based on real-world principles so it at least makes sense.  You can even guess as to what cover might or might not give you an advantage, and where you would be safe. On the other side of the coin, games like chess have clear cut movement.  But somehow in this game, with a grid like chess, they managed to make the movement and line-of-sight more complicated than warhammer yet less-clear and precise.  This is also seen in forum posts where no one can agree on a few particular mechanics, particularly involving large bases and special abilities like pounce.

5) The last problem with the game is persistent leveling.  When I play a tactical game and lose brutally to a player, it is nice to know we can come back and start again on the same playing field.  That is not really possible in this game.  Which again, I would have no problem with if it was Co-Op or story driven, but this game has a competitive tactical edge which makes the whole concept of persistent leveling stressful.

In summary, I feel like this game tries to weave story into what is otherwise a tactical game, which causes both the story and tactics to suffer.  There are better story games out there and better tactical games out there, don't waste your time with this one.
